‭parameters.‬‭In‬‭the‬‭case‬‭where‬‭the‬‭loss‬‭function‬‭is‬‭given‬‭by‬‭the‬‭negative‬‭log-likelihood,‬‭the‬
‭parameter‬‭estimates‬‭that‬‭minimize‬‭the‬‭loss‬‭are‬‭the‬‭maximum‬‭likelihood‬‭estimates‬‭(MLE’s)‬
‭(Bishop 2006)‬‭.‬
‭2.1.4 Gradient Descent‬
‭The‬‭parameter‬‭estimates‬‭that‬‭minimize‬‭the‬‭loss‬‭function‬‭can‬‭be‬‭found‬‭by‬‭setting‬‭the‬‭gradient‬
‭of the loss function equal to zero and solving for the parameters:‬
‭(2.11)‬
‭However,‬‭this‬‭equation‬‭is‬‭unfeasible‬‭to‬‭solve‬‭analytically.‬‭Instead‬‭a‬‭numerical‬‭method‬‭such‬
‭as‬‭gradient‬‭descent‬‭can‬‭be‬‭used.‬‭In‬‭gradient‬‭descent‬‭the‬‭parameters‬‭are‬‭initialized‬‭to‬‭some‬
‭value‬‭and‬‭then‬‭the‬‭algorithm‬‭moves‬‭through‬‭the‬‭parameter‬‭space‬‭in‬‭a‬‭succession‬‭of‬‭small‬
‭steps‬‭in‬‭the‬‭direction‬‭of‬‭the‬‭negative‬‭gradient,‬‭which‬‭is‬‭the‬‭direction‬‭that‬‭the‬‭loss‬‭function‬
‭decreases the most quickly:‬
‭(2.12)‬
‭The‬‭parameter‬
‭is‬‭known‬‭as‬‭the‬‭learning‬‭rate‬‭and‬
‭labels‬‭the‬‭iteration‬‭step‬‭(Bishop‬
‭2006)‬‭.‬
‭In‬‭the‬‭standard‬‭form‬‭of‬‭gradient‬‭descent‬‭the‬‭entire‬‭training‬‭set‬‭is‬‭used‬‭to‬‭evaluate‬
‭in‬
‭order‬ ‭to‬ ‭take‬ ‭a‬ ‭step.‬ ‭A‬ ‭more‬ ‭efficient‬ ‭method‬ ‭known‬ ‭as‬ ‭mini-batch‬ ‭stochastic‬ ‭gradient‬
‭descent‬‭is‬‭to‬‭take‬‭a‬‭random‬‭sample‬‭of‬‭the‬‭training‬‭data‬‭and‬‭use‬‭that‬‭to‬‭approximate‬
‭.‬
‭The‬ ‭random‬ ‭sample‬ ‭is‬ ‭called‬ ‭a‬ ‭mini-batch‬ ‭with‬ ‭the‬ ‭entire‬‭training‬‭dataset‬‭being‬‭a‬‭batch.‬
‭Different‬ ‭variants‬ ‭of‬ ‭stochastic‬ ‭gradient‬ ‭descent‬ ‭exist‬ ‭and‬ ‭are‬ ‭implemented‬‭in‬‭various‬‭so‬
‭called‬ ‭optimizers‬ ‭that‬ ‭control‬ ‭how‬ ‭the‬ ‭parameters‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭network‬ ‭are‬ ‭updated‬ ‭using‬ ‭the‬
‭gradient‬‭of‬‭the‬‭loss‬‭function.‬‭(Chollet‬‭2018)‬‭The‬‭Adam‬‭optimizer‬‭(Kingma‬‭and‬‭Ba‬‭2014)‬‭for‬
‭example‬‭implements‬‭a‬‭variant‬‭of‬‭stochastic‬‭gradient‬‭descent‬‭where‬‭individual‬‭learning‬‭rates‬
‭are‬‭used‬‭for‬‭the‬‭different‬‭parameters‬‭and‬‭where‬‭the‬‭learning‬‭rates‬‭are‬‭updated‬‭throughout‬
‭the gradient descent based on estimates of the first and second moments of the gradient.‬
‭12‬
==PAGE:17==
‭2.1.5 Forward and Backward propagation‬
‭We need a way to calculate the gradient of the loss function. This is taken care of with the‬
‭so-called forward and backward propagation.‬
‭In forward propagation an input‬
‭is given to the neural network and as information passes‬
‭through the network all values of variables on its path are stored.‬
‭Backward propagation or backpropagation calculates the partial derivatives of the loss‬
‭function with respect to all the different parameters by traversing the neural network in‬
‭reverse order according to the chain rule from calculus:‬
‭(2.13)‬
‭Values of variables from the forward pass are here used to evaluate the different partial‬
‭derivatives‬‭(A. Zhang et al. 2021)‬‭.‬
‭2.1.6 Training Neural Networks‬
‭When‬‭training‬‭a‬‭neural‬‭network‬‭the‬‭weights‬‭and‬‭biases‬‭are‬‭first‬‭initialized,‬‭either‬‭by‬‭setting‬
‭them‬‭to‬‭specific‬‭values‬‭or‬‭by‬‭drawing‬‭them‬‭from‬‭a‬‭distribution.‬‭For‬‭standard‬‭gradient‬‭descent‬
‭the following four steps are then repeated, until the loss seems sufficiently low:‬
‭1.‬ ‭Feed‬‭all‬‭observations‬‭of‬‭the‬‭input‬‭feature‬
‭in‬‭the‬‭training‬‭data‬‭to‬‭the‬‭neural‬‭network‬
‭in a forward pass.‬
‭2.‬ ‭Compute‬ ‭the‬ ‭loss‬ ‭from‬ ‭all‬ ‭output‬ ‭variables‬
‭and‬ ‭all‬ ‭observations‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭target‬
‭feature‬
‭.‬
‭3.‬ ‭Compute the gradient of the loss function‬
‭in a backward pass.‬
‭4.‬ ‭Apply change to weights and biases by gradient descent (equation 2.12).‬
‭Each‬‭iteration,‬‭where‬‭output‬‭variables‬
‭for‬‭the‬‭entire‬‭training‬‭data‬‭are‬‭computed,‬‭is‬‭called‬
‭an‬‭epoch.‬‭The‬‭first‬‭epoch‬‭thus‬‭ends‬‭after‬‭the‬‭first‬‭forward‬‭pass‬‭(step‬‭1)‬‭and‬‭the‬‭second‬‭after‬
‭the second forward pass and so on.‬
‭If‬‭we‬‭are‬‭using‬‭mini-batch‬‭stochastic‬‭gradient‬‭descent,‬‭the‬‭training‬‭data‬‭is‬‭at‬‭the‬‭start‬‭of‬‭an‬
‭epoch‬‭randomly‬‭split‬‭into‬‭mini-batches‬‭of‬‭approximately‬‭equal‬‭size.‬‭Step‬‭1-4‬‭are‬‭then‬‭applied‬
‭to‬‭each‬‭mini-batch.‬‭An‬‭epoch‬‭ends‬‭when‬‭all‬‭mini-batches‬‭have‬‭been‬‭through‬‭the‬‭steps‬‭(only‬
‭step‬ ‭1‬ ‭for‬ ‭the‬ ‭first‬‭epoch)‬‭and‬‭when‬‭output‬‭variables‬
‭for‬‭the‬‭entire‬‭training‬‭data‬‭have‬
‭been computed‬‭(Chollet‬‭2018)‬‭.‬
‭2.2 Generalization‬
‭When‬‭training‬‭a‬‭model,‬‭we‬‭do‬‭not‬‭want‬‭it‬‭to‬‭only‬‭perform‬‭well‬‭on‬‭the‬‭training‬‭data,‬‭but‬‭to‬
‭perform‬‭well‬‭in‬‭general.‬‭That‬‭is‬‭it‬‭should‬‭perform‬‭well‬‭on‬‭previously‬‭unobserved‬‭data.‬‭This‬‭is‬
‭what‬‭is‬‭called‬‭generalization.‬‭One‬‭way‬‭to‬‭evaluate‬‭the‬‭performance‬‭on‬‭unobserved‬‭data‬‭is‬‭by‬
‭the‬ ‭holdout‬ ‭method,‬‭where‬‭the‬‭full‬‭dataset‬‭is‬‭randomly‬‭split‬‭into‬‭two;‬‭a‬‭training‬‭set‬‭and‬‭a‬
‭validation‬ ‭set.‬ ‭The‬ ‭model‬ ‭is‬ ‭then‬‭trained‬‭on‬‭the‬‭training‬‭set‬‭and‬‭the‬‭loss‬‭is‬‭in‬‭addition‬‭to‬
‭being‬‭computed‬‭on‬‭the‬‭training‬‭set‬‭also‬‭computed‬‭on‬‭the‬‭validation‬‭set.‬‭The‬‭loss‬‭computed‬
‭13‬
==PAGE:18==
‭on‬ ‭the‬ ‭validation‬ ‭set‬ ‭gives‬ ‭an‬ ‭estimate‬ ‭for‬ ‭how‬ ‭well‬ ‭the‬ ‭model‬ ‭generalizes‬ ‭to‬ ‭all‬ ‭future‬
‭unobserved data.‬
‭To‬‭understand‬‭how‬‭the‬‭training‬‭loss‬‭and‬‭validation‬‭loss‬‭differs‬‭during‬‭training,‬‭let‬‭us‬‭look‬‭at‬
‭an‬ ‭example‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭holdout‬ ‭method‬ ‭used‬ ‭to‬‭train‬‭a‬‭neural‬‭network‬‭that‬‭works‬‭as‬‭a‬‭binary‬
‭classifier.‬‭We‬‭have‬‭two‬‭classes;‬‭A‬‭and‬‭B.‬‭The‬‭neural‬‭network‬‭takes‬‭two‬‭input‬‭variables‬
‭and‬
‭and‬‭outputs‬‭the‬‭predicted‬‭probability‬‭of‬ ‭belonging‬‭to‬‭class‬‭A.‬‭The‬‭training‬‭loss‬‭and‬
‭validation‬‭loss‬‭can‬‭be‬‭plotted‬‭as‬‭a‬‭function‬‭of‬‭the‬‭epoch.‬‭This‬‭is‬‭known‬‭as‬‭learning‬‭curves.‬
‭Idealised‬‭learning‬‭curves‬‭are‬‭shown‬‭in‬‭fig.‬‭2.3.‬‭In‬‭the‬‭start‬‭when‬‭the‬‭model‬‭is‬‭trained,‬‭it‬‭will‬
‭underfit‬‭-‬‭that‬‭is‬‭it‬‭has‬‭not‬‭learned‬‭the‬‭relevant‬‭patterns‬‭of‬‭the‬‭training‬‭data‬‭(fig.‬‭2.4a).‬‭At‬‭this‬
‭point‬‭in‬‭the‬‭learning‬‭process‬‭both‬‭the‬‭validation‬‭loss‬‭and‬‭training‬‭loss‬‭will‬‭decrease.‬‭They‬‭will‬
‭decrease‬‭until‬‭the‬‭validation‬‭loss‬‭reaches‬‭a‬‭minimum.‬‭This‬‭is‬‭where‬‭the‬‭model‬‭will‬‭generalize‬
‭the‬‭best‬‭-‬‭it‬‭has‬‭reached‬‭a‬‭robust‬‭fit‬‭(fig.‬‭2.4b).‬‭If‬‭we‬‭keep‬‭training,‬‭the‬‭validation‬‭loss‬‭will‬
‭start‬‭to‬‭increase‬‭while‬‭the‬‭training‬‭loss‬‭still‬‭will‬‭decrease.‬‭The‬‭model‬‭is‬‭starting‬‭to‬‭overfit‬‭and‬
‭beginning‬‭to‬‭fit‬‭the‬‭noise‬‭of‬‭the‬‭training‬‭data‬‭(fig.‬‭2.4c).‬‭If‬‭the‬‭model‬‭is‬‭flexible‬‭enough,‬‭which‬
‭is‬ ‭the‬ ‭case‬ ‭for‬ ‭neural‬ ‭networks‬ ‭with‬ ‭many‬ ‭hidden‬ ‭neurons,‬ ‭it‬ ‭could‬ ‭even‬ ‭end‬ ‭up‬ ‭just‬
‭memorizing what the output should be for different inputs.‬
‭Fig.‬ ‭2.3.‬ ‭Idealised‬ ‭learning‬ ‭curves.‬ ‭Training‬ ‭loss‬ ‭and‬ ‭validation‬ ‭loss‬ ‭are‬ ‭shown‬ ‭as‬ ‭a‬‭function‬‭of‬‭epoch.‬‭It‬‭is‬
‭shown when underfitting and overfitting occurs. A robust fit is marked with a vertical line.‬
‭Fig.‬ ‭2.4.‬ ‭Underfitting‬ ‭and‬ ‭overfitting‬ ‭for‬ ‭a‬ ‭binary‬ ‭classifier.‬ ‭Contour‬ ‭plots‬ ‭are‬ ‭shown‬ ‭for‬ ‭a‬ ‭neural‬ ‭network‬
‭working‬‭as‬‭a‬‭binary‬‭classifier‬‭with‬‭classes‬‭A‬‭and‬‭B.‬‭On‬‭the‬‭first‬‭and‬‭second‬‭axis‬‭we‬‭have‬‭the‬‭inputs‬‭to‬‭the‬‭neural‬
‭network‬
‭and‬
‭.‬‭The‬‭color‬‭inside‬‭contours‬‭indicates‬‭the‬‭value‬‭of‬‭the‬‭predicted‬‭probability‬‭of‬‭belonging‬‭to‬‭class‬
‭A‬‭for‬‭various‬‭inputs‬‭to‬‭the‬‭neural‬‭network.‬‭Dark‬‭red‬‭means‬‭a‬‭high‬‭probability‬‭of‬‭belonging‬‭to‬‭class‬‭A,‬‭white‬‭means‬
‭there‬ ‭is‬ ‭a‬ ‭probability‬ ‭of‬ ‭0.5‬ ‭and‬ ‭dark‬ ‭blue‬ ‭means‬ ‭that‬ ‭the‬ ‭probability‬ ‭is‬ ‭low.‬‭Points‬‭are‬‭observations,‬‭with‬‭red‬
‭points‬‭belonging‬‭to‬‭class‬‭A‬‭and‬‭blue‬‭points‬‭belonging‬‭to‬‭class‬‭B.‬‭Circular‬‭points‬‭are‬‭training‬‭samples‬‭while‬‭star‬
‭shaped‬ ‭points‬ ‭are‬ ‭validation‬ ‭samples.‬ ‭The‬ ‭figure‬ ‭is‬ ‭a‬ ‭modified‬ ‭version‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭one‬ ‭found‬ ‭in‬ ‭(Horn‬ ‭2021)‬‭.‬ ‭(a)‬
‭Underfitting.‬‭This‬‭plot‬‭illustrates‬‭when‬‭the‬‭classifier‬‭is‬‭underfitting.‬‭(b)‬‭Robust‬‭fit.‬‭This‬‭plot‬‭illustrates‬‭a‬‭robust‬‭fit‬
‭for the classifier.‬‭(c) Overfitting.‬‭This plot illustrates when the classifier is overfitting.‬
‭When‬‭developing‬‭a‬‭model,‬‭we‬‭are‬‭normally‬‭tuning‬‭its‬‭configuration,‬‭such‬‭as‬‭the‬‭number‬‭of‬
‭layers‬‭and‬‭number‬‭of‬‭hidden‬‭neurons‬‭in‬‭order‬‭to‬‭find‬‭which‬‭configuration‬‭performs‬‭the‬‭best.‬
‭The‬‭parameters‬‭of‬‭a‬‭configuration‬‭are‬‭called‬‭hyperparameters‬‭to‬‭distinguish‬‭them‬‭from‬‭the‬
‭14‬
==PAGE:19==
‭parameters,‬‭which‬‭in‬‭the‬‭case‬‭of‬‭neural‬‭networks‬‭are‬‭the‬‭weights‬‭and‬‭biases.‬‭The‬‭selection‬
‭of‬‭the‬‭best‬‭hyperparameters‬‭is‬‭done‬‭using‬‭the‬‭validation‬‭set,‬‭because‬‭we‬‭are‬‭interested‬‭in‬
‭that‬‭the‬‭model‬‭performs‬‭well‬‭on‬‭unobserved‬‭data.‬‭In‬‭essence,‬‭the‬‭tuning‬‭of‬‭hyperparameters‬
‭is‬‭a‬‭form‬‭of‬‭learning,‬‭where‬‭we‬‭search‬‭for‬‭a‬‭good‬‭set‬‭of‬‭hyperparameters‬‭in‬‭the‬‭space‬‭of‬‭all‬
‭possible‬ ‭hyperparameters.‬ ‭Therefore,‬ ‭when‬ ‭we‬ ‭use‬ ‭the‬ ‭validation‬ ‭set‬ ‭to‬ ‭select‬ ‭the‬
‭hyperparameters,‬‭we‬‭can‬‭end‬‭up‬‭overfitting‬‭on‬‭the‬‭validation‬‭set‬‭just‬‭as‬‭we‬‭could‬‭overfit‬‭to‬
‭the‬ ‭training‬ ‭set‬ ‭when‬ ‭learning‬ ‭the‬ ‭parameters‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭model.‬ ‭This‬ ‭means‬ ‭that‬‭we‬‭have‬‭a‬
‭model‬‭that‬‭performs‬‭artificially‬‭well‬‭on‬‭the‬‭validation‬‭set.‬‭But‬‭what‬‭we‬‭are‬‭interested‬‭in‬‭is‬‭that‬
‭the‬‭model‬‭should‬‭perform‬‭well‬‭in‬‭general.‬‭The‬‭solution‬‭to‬‭this‬‭is‬‭to‬‭evaluate‬‭the‬‭performance‬
‭on‬‭yet‬‭another‬‭independent‬‭dataset‬‭-‬‭the‬‭test‬‭set.‬‭This‬‭means‬‭that‬‭instead‬‭of‬‭dividing‬‭the‬‭full‬
‭dataset‬ ‭into‬ ‭two,‬ ‭it‬ ‭is‬ ‭divided‬ ‭into‬ ‭three;‬ ‭a‬ ‭training,‬ ‭validation‬ ‭and‬ ‭test‬‭set‬‭(Chollet‬‭2018;‬
‭Herlau, Mørup, and Schmidt 2019)‬‭.‬
‭2.2.1 Cross Validation‬
‭The‬‭holdout‬‭method‬‭already‬‭described‬‭is‬‭the‬‭simplest‬‭form‬‭of‬‭what‬‭is‬‭called‬‭cross‬‭validation‬‭-‬
‭a‬‭framework‬‭for‬‭estimating‬‭how‬‭well‬‭a‬‭model‬‭will‬‭generalize‬‭to‬‭future‬‭unobserved‬‭data.‬‭The‬
‭holdout‬ ‭method‬ ‭has‬ ‭one‬ ‭major‬ ‭flaw‬ ‭-‬ ‭it‬ ‭does‬ ‭not‬‭utilize‬‭the‬‭dataset‬‭very‬‭well,‬‭which‬‭is‬‭a‬
‭problem‬‭if‬‭the‬‭dataset‬‭is‬‭small.‬‭To‬‭get‬‭a‬‭good‬‭estimate‬‭of‬‭how‬‭a‬‭model‬‭generalizes‬‭to‬‭unseen‬
‭data‬‭the‬‭test‬‭and‬‭validation‬‭set‬‭should‬‭both‬‭be‬‭large.‬‭But‬‭we‬‭do‬‭not‬‭wish‬‭to‬‭make‬‭the‬‭training‬
‭set‬‭small‬‭either,‬‭because‬‭then‬‭there‬‭is‬‭a‬‭good‬‭chance‬‭that‬‭the‬‭model‬‭does‬‭not‬‭capture‬‭the‬
‭signal‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭data.‬ ‭The‬ ‭solution‬ ‭to‬ ‭this‬ ‭dilemma‬ ‭is‬ ‭to‬‭use‬‭more‬‭advanced‬‭forms‬‭of‬‭cross‬
‭validation such as K-fold cross validation and nested K*L-fold cross validation.‬
‭In‬ ‭K-fold‬ ‭cross‬ ‭validation‬ ‭(fig.‬ ‭2.5a)‬ ‭the‬ ‭full‬ ‭dataset‬ ‭is‬ ‭randomly‬ ‭split‬ ‭into‬
‭partitions‬‭of‬
‭approximately‬‭equal‬‭size.‬‭For‬‭each‬‭fold‬
‭the‬‭model‬‭is‬‭evaluated‬‭on‬‭partition‬
‭,‬‭being‬‭the‬
‭validation‬‭set‬‭and‬‭trained‬‭on‬‭the‬‭remaining‬
‭partitions,‬‭constituting‬‭a‬‭training‬‭set.‬‭This‬
‭gives‬
‭estimates‬‭for‬‭how‬‭the‬‭model‬‭generalizes‬‭to‬‭unseen‬‭data,‬‭which‬‭can‬‭be‬‭combined‬‭to‬
‭one estimate. In this way the full data set is used to evaluate how well the model generalizes.‬
‭Like‬‭it‬‭was‬‭the‬‭case‬‭for‬‭the‬‭holdout‬‭method,‬‭to‬‭be‬‭able‬‭to‬‭both‬‭tune‬‭the‬‭hyperparameters‬‭of‬‭a‬
‭model‬‭and‬‭evaluate‬‭the‬‭performance‬‭of‬‭the‬‭tuned‬‭model‬‭we‬‭need‬‭the‬‭test‬‭set‬‭as‬‭well.‬‭This‬‭is‬
‭where‬‭nested‬‭K*L-fold‬‭cross‬‭validation‬‭comes‬‭into‬‭the‬‭picture‬‭(fig.‬‭2.5b).‬‭In‬‭this‬‭method‬‭the‬
‭full‬‭dataset‬‭is‬‭randomly‬‭split‬‭into‬
‭partitions‬‭of‬‭approximately‬‭equal‬‭size.‬‭The‬‭method‬‭has‬
‭an‬‭outer‬‭loop‬‭where‬‭for‬‭each‬‭outer‬‭fold‬
‭partition‬
‭is‬‭used‬‭as‬‭the‬‭test‬‭set.‬‭In‬‭the‬‭inner‬‭loop‬
‭of‬‭L‬‭folds,‬‭partition‬
‭of‬‭the‬‭remaining‬
‭partitions‬‭is‬‭used‬‭as‬‭the‬‭validation‬‭set‬‭and‬‭the‬
‭remaining‬
‭partitions‬‭are‬‭used‬‭as‬‭the‬‭training‬‭set‬‭(Chollet‬‭2018;‬‭Herlau,‬‭Mørup,‬‭and‬
‭Schmidt 2019)‬‭.‬
‭15‬
==PAGE:20==
‭Fig.‬‭2.5.‬‭Cross-validation.‬‭A‬‭schematic‬‭of‬‭how‬‭a‬‭dataset‬‭is‬‭partitioned‬‭is‬‭shown‬‭for‬‭two‬‭types‬‭of‬‭cross-validation.‬
‭(a)‬‭K-fold‬‭cross-validation.‬‭Cross-validation‬‭with‬
‭folds‬‭is‬‭illustrated.‬‭The‬‭index‬
‭of‬‭each‬‭of‬‭the‬‭five‬‭folds‬
‭are‬ ‭shown‬ ‭inside‬ ‭red‬ ‭empty‬ ‭boxes‬ ‭at‬ ‭the‬ ‭bottom.‬ ‭The‬ ‭full‬‭dataset‬‭is‬‭split‬‭into‬‭five‬‭partitions.‬‭For‬‭each‬‭fold‬‭the‬
‭partitioning‬‭of‬‭the‬‭dataset‬‭is‬‭shown‬‭as‬‭filled‬‭boxes‬‭in‬‭a‬‭column,‬‭with‬‭each‬‭box‬‭being‬‭a‬‭partition‬‭and‬‭the‬‭column‬‭of‬
‭filled‬ ‭boxes‬ ‭being‬ ‭the‬ ‭full‬ ‭dataset.‬ ‭The‬ ‭color‬ ‭of‬ ‭a‬ ‭filled‬ ‭box‬ ‭indicates‬ ‭if‬ ‭the‬ ‭partition‬ ‭belongs‬ ‭to‬ ‭the‬‭training‬‭or‬
‭validation‬‭set‬‭in‬‭the‬‭given‬‭fold.‬‭(b)‬‭Nested‬‭K*L-fold‬‭cross-validation.‬‭Nested‬‭cross-validation‬‭with‬
‭outer‬
‭folds‬ ‭and‬
‭inner‬ ‭folds‬‭is‬‭illustrated.‬‭Red‬‭empty‬‭boxes‬‭at‬‭the‬‭bottom‬‭contain‬‭the‬‭index‬
‭of‬‭the‬‭outer‬‭fold,‬
‭while‬‭blue‬‭empty‬‭boxes‬‭contain‬‭the‬‭index‬
‭of‬‭the‬‭inner‬‭fold.‬‭The‬‭full‬‭dataset‬‭is‬‭split‬‭into‬‭five‬‭partitions.‬‭For‬‭each‬
‭combination‬‭of‬‭inner‬‭and‬‭outer‬‭folds‬‭the‬‭partitioning‬‭of‬‭the‬‭dataset‬‭is‬‭shown‬‭as‬‭filled‬‭boxes‬‭in‬‭a‬‭column,‬‭with‬‭each‬
‭box‬‭being‬‭a‬‭partition‬‭and‬‭the‬‭column‬‭of‬‭filled‬‭boxes‬‭being‬‭the‬‭full‬‭dataset.‬‭The‬‭color‬‭of‬‭a‬‭filled‬‭box‬‭indicates‬‭if‬‭the‬
‭partition belongs to the training, validation or test set in the given combination of folds.‬
‭2.2.2 Improving Generalization‬
‭Many‬ ‭techniques‬ ‭exist‬ ‭to‬ ‭improve‬‭generalization.‬‭In‬‭the‬‭following,‬‭techniques‬‭used‬‭in‬‭this‬
‭project will be presented.‬
‭In‬‭fig.‬‭2.3,‬‭it‬‭was‬‭seen‬‭how‬‭a‬‭model‬‭generalizes‬‭the‬‭best‬‭when‬‭the‬‭validation‬‭loss‬‭reaches‬‭a‬
‭minimum.‬‭A‬‭technique‬‭known‬‭as‬‭early‬‭stopping‬‭will‬‭stop‬‭training‬‭when‬‭there‬‭has‬‭been‬‭no‬
‭improvement‬ ‭in‬ ‭a‬ ‭performance‬ ‭metric‬ ‭of‬ ‭interest‬ ‭(e.g.‬ ‭loss)‬ ‭after‬ ‭a‬ ‭specified‬ ‭number‬ ‭of‬
‭epochs‬‭called‬‭the‬‭patience.‬‭The‬‭best‬‭known‬‭model‬‭state‬‭will‬‭be‬‭remembered.‬‭Early‬‭stopping‬
‭belongs‬‭to‬‭the‬‭class‬‭of‬‭techniques‬‭called‬‭regularization,‬‭where‬‭a‬‭model's‬‭ability‬‭to‬‭fit‬‭perfectly‬
‭to‬‭the‬‭training‬‭data‬‭is‬‭actively‬‭inhibited‬‭in‬‭order‬‭to‬‭reduce‬‭the‬‭complexity‬‭of‬‭the‬‭model‬‭and‬
‭make it generalize better‬‭(Chollet‬‭2018)‬‭.‬
‭In‬ ‭a‬ ‭different‬ ‭technique‬ ‭called‬ ‭ensemble‬ ‭averaging,‬ ‭multiple‬ ‭models‬ ‭are‬ ‭trained‬ ‭and‬
‭predictions‬ ‭for‬ ‭the‬ ‭ensemble‬ ‭of‬ ‭models‬ ‭is‬ ‭computed‬ ‭as‬ ‭the‬ ‭average‬ ‭over‬ ‭predictions‬ ‭of‬
‭individual‬ ‭models.‬ ‭Often,‬ ‭the‬ ‭ensemble‬ ‭of‬ ‭models‬ ‭will‬ ‭perform‬ ‭better‬ ‭in‬ ‭general‬ ‭than‬‭the‬
‭individual‬‭models,‬‭since‬‭the‬‭various‬‭errors‬‭of‬‭the‬‭individual‬‭models‬‭average‬‭out‬‭(Fernandes‬
‭and Vinga 2016)‬‭.‬
‭16‬
==PAGE:21==
‭Dropout‬‭is‬‭another‬‭regularization‬‭technique.‬‭Applying‬‭dropout‬‭to‬‭a‬‭layer‬‭of‬‭a‬‭neural‬‭network,‬
‭consists‬ ‭of‬ ‭randomly‬ ‭dropping‬ ‭out‬‭neurons‬‭in‬‭the‬‭layer‬‭during‬‭training,‬‭meaning‬‭that‬‭their‬
‭output‬ ‭is‬ ‭set‬ ‭to‬ ‭zero.‬ ‭The‬ ‭fraction‬ ‭of‬ ‭neurons‬ ‭that‬ ‭are‬ ‭dropped‬ ‭out‬ ‭is‬ ‭specified‬ ‭with‬ ‭the‬
‭dropout‬‭rate‬ ‭.‬‭The‬‭neurons‬‭that‬‭are‬‭randomly‬‭selected‬‭change‬‭each‬‭weight‬‭update‬‭cycle.‬
‭Dropout‬‭is‬‭not‬‭applied‬‭during‬‭validation‬‭or‬‭testing.‬‭To‬‭balance‬‭for‬‭the‬‭fact‬‭that‬‭more‬‭neurons‬
‭are‬‭active‬‭during‬‭validation‬‭and‬‭testing,‬‭the‬‭layer‬‭outputs‬‭are‬‭scaled‬‭up‬‭doing‬‭training‬‭with‬‭a‬
‭factor‬
‭.‬‭This‬‭ensures‬‭that‬‭the‬‭average‬‭output‬‭of‬‭the‬‭layer‬‭remains‬‭the‬‭same‬‭as‬‭if‬‭dropout‬
‭was‬ ‭not‬ ‭applied.‬ ‭Dropping‬ ‭out‬ ‭neurons‬‭corresponds‬‭to‬‭that‬‭they‬‭are‬‭temporarily‬‭removed‬
‭from‬‭the‬‭network‬‭(Chollet‬‭2018;‬‭Géron‬‭2019;‬‭A.‬‭Zhang‬‭et‬‭al.‬‭2021)‬‭.‬‭The‬‭theory‬‭behind‬‭why‬
‭dropout‬‭works‬‭is‬‭that‬‭when‬‭training‬‭a‬‭neural‬‭network,‬‭some‬‭neurons‬‭might‬‭learn‬‭to‬‭fix‬‭the‬
‭mistake‬‭of‬‭other‬‭neurons,‬‭which‬‭can‬‭lead‬‭to‬‭complex‬‭co-adaptations‬‭specific‬‭to‬‭the‬‭training‬
‭data.‬‭This‬‭means‬‭overfitting,‬‭since‬‭the‬‭complex‬‭co-adaptations‬‭fail‬‭to‬‭generalize‬‭to‬‭unseen‬
‭data.‬‭By‬‭removing‬‭neurons‬‭randomly‬‭in‬‭dropout,‬‭each‬‭neuron‬‭must‬‭learn‬‭to‬‭be‬‭as‬‭useful‬‭as‬
‭possible‬‭on‬‭its‬‭own,‬‭since‬‭it‬‭cannot‬‭rely‬‭on‬‭the‬‭other‬‭neurons‬‭to‬‭fix‬‭up‬‭its‬‭mistakes.‬‭This‬‭leads‬
‭to‬‭better‬‭generalization‬‭(Géron‬‭2019;‬‭Yadav‬‭2022)‬‭.‬‭Another‬‭way‬‭to‬‭look‬‭at‬‭dropout‬‭is‬‭to‬‭see‬‭it‬
‭as‬‭a‬‭cheap‬‭approximation‬‭to‬‭ensemble‬‭averaging‬‭of‬‭many‬‭neural‬‭networks,‬‭since‬‭removing‬
‭different‬ ‭neurons‬ ‭from‬ ‭the‬ ‭base‬ ‭network‬ ‭at‬ ‭each‬ ‭weight‬ ‭update‬ ‭cycle‬ ‭gives‬ ‭different‬
‭subnetworks,‬ ‭which‬ ‭all‬ ‭contribute‬ ‭to‬ ‭learning‬ ‭the‬ ‭parameters‬ ‭of‬‭the‬‭base‬‭network‬‭(Géron‬
‭2019; Goodfellow, Bengio, and Courville 2016)‬‭.‬
‭A‬‭technique‬‭known‬‭as‬‭loss‬‭weighting,‬‭tries‬‭to‬‭mitigate‬‭overfitting‬‭to‬‭the‬‭majority‬‭classes‬‭of‬‭an‬
‭imbalanced‬‭training‬‭set.‬‭Overfitting‬‭to‬‭the‬‭majority‬‭class‬‭means‬‭that‬‭the‬‭model‬‭will‬‭perform‬
‭poorly‬ ‭on‬ ‭the‬ ‭minority‬ ‭classes.‬ ‭The‬ ‭technique‬ ‭works‬ ‭by‬ ‭weighting‬ ‭observations‬ ‭from‬ ‭the‬
‭minority‬ ‭classes‬ ‭higher‬ ‭in‬ ‭the‬ ‭loss‬ ‭function‬ ‭(equation‬ ‭2.10)‬ ‭than‬ ‭those‬ ‭from‬ ‭the‬ ‭majority‬
‭classes.‬ ‭That‬ ‭is,‬ ‭the‬ ‭loss‬ ‭contribution‬
‭from‬ ‭each‬ ‭observation‬ ‭in‬ ‭the‬ ‭training‬ ‭set‬‭is‬
‭scaled by a weight‬
‭whose value depends on what class‬
‭the observation‬‭belongs to:‬
‭(2.14)‬
‭Minority‬‭classes‬‭get‬‭a‬‭larger‬‭weight,‬‭while‬‭minority‬‭classes‬‭get‬‭a‬‭smaller‬‭weight.‬‭This‬‭way‬‭it‬
‭becomes‬ ‭more‬ ‭important‬ ‭for‬ ‭the‬ ‭model‬ ‭to‬ ‭do‬ ‭well‬ ‭on‬ ‭the‬ ‭minority‬ ‭class‬ ‭(Tantai‬ ‭2023;‬
‭Shrivastava 2020)‬‭.‬
‭One‬‭very‬‭important‬‭step‬‭before‬‭starting‬‭to‬‭train‬‭a‬‭model‬‭is‬‭to‬‭reduce‬‭redundancy‬‭in‬‭the‬‭data,‬
‭meaning‬‭that‬‭for‬‭a‬‭given‬‭observation‬‭similar‬‭observations‬‭are‬‭removed.‬‭If‬‭a‬‭model‬‭is‬‭trained‬
‭on‬‭a‬‭dataset‬‭with‬‭many‬‭redundant‬‭observations‬‭it‬‭will‬‭overfit‬‭to‬‭data‬‭that‬‭is‬‭repeated‬‭and‬‭not‬
‭learn‬‭the‬‭minority‬‭patterns‬‭of‬‭the‬‭dataset.‬‭This‬‭means‬‭poor‬‭generalization.‬‭Another‬‭just‬‭as‬
‭important‬‭point‬‭is‬‭that‬‭the‬‭performance‬‭of‬‭the‬‭model‬‭will‬‭be‬‭overestimated‬‭when‬‭evaluated‬‭on‬
‭the‬ ‭validation‬ ‭or‬ ‭test‬ ‭set,‬ ‭since‬ ‭they‬ ‭will‬ ‭be‬ ‭very‬ ‭similar‬ ‭to‬ ‭the‬ ‭training‬ ‭set‬ ‭due‬ ‭to‬ ‭the‬
‭redundancy‬‭(Lund et al. 2005)‬‭.‬
‭For‬‭sequence‬‭data‬‭multiple‬‭algorithms‬‭for‬‭redundancy‬‭reduction‬‭exist.‬‭The‬‭one‬‭used‬‭in‬‭this‬
‭project‬‭is‬‭the‬‭Hobohm‬‭1‬‭algorithm‬‭(Hobohm‬‭et‬‭al.‬‭1992)‬‭.‬‭The‬‭algorithm‬‭takes‬‭an‬‭input‬‭list‬‭of‬
‭sequences‬‭and‬‭one‬‭by‬‭one‬‭adds‬‭the‬‭sequences‬‭to‬‭a‬‭non-redundant‬‭list‬‭if‬‭they‬‭are‬‭not‬‭similar‬
‭to‬ ‭the‬‭ones‬‭that‬‭have‬‭already‬‭been‬‭added.‬‭The‬‭first‬‭sequence‬‭is‬‭always‬‭added.‬‭The‬‭next‬
‭ones‬ ‭are‬ ‭checked‬ ‭against‬ ‭all‬ ‭sequences‬ ‭in‬ ‭the‬ ‭non-redundant‬ ‭list.‬ ‭If‬ ‭the‬ ‭similarity‬ ‭of‬ ‭a‬
‭candidate‬‭sequence‬‭to‬‭all‬‭sequences‬‭in‬‭the‬‭non-redundant‬‭list‬‭is‬‭below‬‭a‬‭specified‬‭threshold,‬
‭17‬
==PAGE:22==
‭the‬‭candidate‬‭sequence‬‭will‬‭be‬‭added‬‭to‬‭the‬‭non-redundant‬‭list,‬‭else‬‭it‬‭will‬‭be‬‭discarded.‬‭Due‬
‭to‬ ‭the‬ ‭non-redundant‬ ‭list‬ ‭being‬ ‭shortest‬ ‭when‬ ‭sequences‬ ‭from‬ ‭top‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭input‬ ‭list‬ ‭are‬
‭compared‬ ‭to‬ ‭it,‬ ‭sequences‬ ‭appearing‬ ‭first‬ ‭in‬ ‭the‬ ‭input‬ ‭list‬ ‭have‬ ‭a‬ ‭lower‬ ‭chance‬‭of‬‭being‬
‭filtered‬‭out.‬‭Therefore,‬‭before‬‭applying‬‭the‬‭Hobohm‬‭1‬‭algorithm‬‭one‬‭can‬‭sort‬‭the‬‭input‬‭list‬
‭according‬‭to‬‭some‬‭property,‬‭where‬‭sequences‬‭with‬‭a‬‭desired‬‭property‬‭appear‬‭at‬‭the‬‭top‬‭of‬
‭the list.‬
‭2.3 Performance Metrics‬
‭Performance metrics are used to evaluate the performance of a model. Even though the loss‬
‭function can be used as a performance metric, one should choose the metric that best aligns‬
‭with the higher-level goals of the model‬‭(Chollet 2018)‬‭. Performance metrics used in the‬
‭project will now be presented.‬
‭2.3.1 AUC‬
‭Given‬‭instances‬‭belonging‬‭to‬‭either‬‭a‬‭positive‬‭or‬‭a‬‭negative‬‭class,‬‭neural‬‭networks‬‭working‬
‭as‬ ‭binary‬ ‭classifiers‬ ‭predict‬ ‭the‬ ‭probability‬ ‭of‬ ‭belonging‬ ‭to‬ ‭the‬ ‭positive‬ ‭class‬ ‭for‬ ‭a‬ ‭given‬
‭instance.‬‭Often‬‭the‬‭higher-level‬‭goal‬‭of‬‭the‬‭classifier‬‭is‬‭to‬‭correctly‬‭classify‬‭instances.‬‭The‬
‭neural‬‭network‬‭does‬‭therefore‬‭not‬‭need‬‭to‬‭be‬‭trained‬‭to‬‭produce‬‭strict‬‭probabilities.‬‭It‬‭would‬
‭be‬‭enough‬‭if‬‭it‬‭just‬‭produced‬‭scores‬‭(values‬‭that‬‭represent‬‭the‬‭degree‬‭to‬‭which‬‭an‬‭instance‬‭is‬
‭a‬‭member‬‭of‬‭a‬‭class),‬‭where‬‭higher‬‭scores‬‭are‬‭assigned‬‭to‬‭the‬‭positive‬‭class.‬‭A‬‭metric‬‭that‬
‭aligns‬‭with‬‭this‬‭higher-level‬‭goal‬‭is‬‭area‬‭under‬‭the‬‭ROC‬‭curve‬‭(AUC),‬‭where‬‭ROC‬‭stands‬‭for‬
‭receiver operating characteristic.‬
‭The‬‭ROC‬‭curve‬‭measures‬‭the‬‭ability‬‭of‬‭a‬‭binary‬‭classifier‬‭to‬‭produce‬‭good‬‭relative‬‭scores.‬
‭That‬‭is‬‭it‬‭measures‬‭the‬‭ability‬‭of‬‭the‬‭classifier‬‭to‬‭give‬‭higher‬‭scores‬‭to‬‭positive‬‭instances‬‭than‬
‭to‬‭negative‬‭ones.‬‭In‬‭order‬‭to‬‭make‬‭a‬‭ROC‬‭curve,‬‭a‬‭scoring‬‭classifier‬‭is‬‭turned‬‭into‬‭discrete‬
‭classifiers (classifiers that only output a class label).‬
‭A‬‭discrete‬‭classifier‬‭has‬‭four‬‭possible‬‭outcomes‬‭when‬‭classifying‬‭an‬‭instance:‬‭If‬‭the‬‭instance‬
‭is‬‭positive‬‭and‬‭it‬‭is‬‭classified‬‭as‬‭so,‬‭it‬‭will‬‭be‬‭counted‬‭as‬‭a‬‭true‬‭positive‬‭(TP),‬‭if‬‭it‬‭instead‬‭is‬
‭classified‬‭as‬‭negative‬‭it‬‭will‬‭be‬‭counted‬‭as‬‭a‬‭false‬‭negative‬‭(FN).‬‭If‬‭the‬‭instance‬‭is‬‭negative‬
‭and‬‭it‬‭is‬‭classified‬‭as‬‭so,‬‭it‬‭will‬‭be‬‭counted‬‭as‬‭a‬‭true‬‭negative‬‭(TN),‬‭if‬‭it‬‭is‬‭instead‬‭classified‬‭as‬
‭positive‬‭it‬‭will‬‭be‬‭counted‬‭as‬‭a‬‭false‬‭positive‬‭(FP).‬‭Given‬‭a‬‭set‬‭of‬‭instances‬‭and‬‭a‬‭discrete‬
‭classifier‬ ‭the‬ ‭count‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭different‬ ‭outcomes‬ ‭can‬ ‭be‬ ‭collected‬ ‭in‬ ‭what‬ ‭is‬ ‭known‬ ‭as‬ ‭a‬
‭confusion‬‭matrix.‬‭Different‬‭metrics‬‭can‬‭be‬‭computed‬‭from‬‭a‬‭confusion‬‭matrix.‬‭Two‬‭of‬‭them,‬
‭used‬ ‭for‬ ‭drawing‬ ‭the‬ ‭ROC‬ ‭curve,‬ ‭are‬ ‭the‬ ‭true‬ ‭positive‬‭rate‬‭(TPR)‬‭and‬‭false‬‭positive‬‭rate‬
‭(FPR).‬ ‭The‬ ‭TPR‬ ‭measures‬ ‭the‬‭fraction‬‭of‬‭actual‬‭positives‬‭correctly‬‭classified‬‭as‬‭positives‬
‭(TP) out of all actual positives (P):‬
‭(2.15)‬
‭The‬‭FPR‬‭on‬‭the‬‭other‬‭hand‬‭measures‬‭the‬‭fraction‬‭of‬‭actual‬‭negatives‬‭incorrectly‬‭classified‬‭as‬
‭positives (FP) out of all actual negatives (N):‬
‭(2.16)‬
‭18‬
==PAGE:23==
‭A‬‭ROC‬‭graph‬‭has‬‭the‬‭FPR‬‭on‬‭its‬‭first‬‭axis‬‭and‬‭the‬‭TPR‬‭on‬‭its‬‭second‬‭axis.‬‭A‬‭ROC‬‭curve‬‭is‬
‭computed‬‭by‬‭varying‬‭the‬‭threshold‬‭of‬‭a‬‭scoring‬‭classifier‬‭for‬‭when‬‭to‬‭classify‬‭an‬‭instance‬‭as‬
‭belonging‬‭to‬‭the‬‭positive‬‭class.‬‭Each‬‭threshold‬‭gives‬‭rise‬‭to‬‭a‬‭discrete‬‭classifier‬‭with‬‭a‬‭FPR‬
‭and a TPR, which can be plotted on the ROC graph as a curve (fig. 2.6).‬
‭A‬‭scoring‬‭classifier‬‭that‬‭performs‬‭perfect‬‭on‬‭a‬‭dataset,‬‭meaning‬‭that‬‭all‬‭positive‬‭instances‬‭are‬
‭scored‬‭higher‬‭than‬‭the‬‭negative‬‭instances,‬‭will‬‭result‬‭in‬‭a‬‭ROC‬‭curve‬‭that‬‭rises‬‭vertically‬‭from‬
‭(0,0)‬‭to‬‭(0,1),‬‭then‬‭horizontally‬‭to‬‭(1,1).‬‭A‬‭scoring‬‭classifier‬‭that‬‭randomly‬‭assigns‬‭scores‬‭to‬
‭instances‬‭will‬‭give‬‭rise‬‭to‬‭a‬‭ROC‬‭curve‬‭that‬‭follows‬‭the‬‭diagonal‬‭line‬
‭.‬‭Scoring‬
‭classifiers‬‭that‬‭do‬‭better‬‭than‬‭random‬‭will‬‭be‬‭above‬‭this‬‭line‬‭and‬‭the‬‭ones‬‭that‬‭do‬‭worse‬‭will‬
‭be below. The better a scoring classifier is, the closer it will hug the point (0,1).‬
‭Fig.‬ ‭2.6.‬ ‭Example‬ ‭of‬ ‭a‬ ‭ROC‬ ‭curve.‬ ‭The‬ ‭plot‬ ‭shows‬ ‭an‬ ‭example‬ ‭of‬ ‭a‬ ‭ROC‬ ‭curve‬ ‭(solid‬ ‭graph).‬ ‭The‬ ‭dashed‬
‭diagonal line depicts what the ROC curve would look like if the classifier was completely random.‬
‭To‬‭be‬‭able‬‭to‬‭easily‬‭compare‬‭various‬‭classifiers‬‭the‬‭ROC‬‭curve‬‭can‬‭be‬‭reduced‬‭to‬‭a‬‭single‬
‭value‬ ‭-‬ ‭the‬ ‭AUC.‬ ‭The‬ ‭AUC‬ ‭is‬ ‭computed‬ ‭as‬ ‭the‬ ‭area‬ ‭under‬ ‭the‬ ‭ROC‬ ‭curve‬ ‭as‬ ‭its‬‭name‬
‭suggests.‬ ‭It‬ ‭is‬ ‭a‬ ‭value‬ ‭between‬ ‭0‬ ‭and‬ ‭1,‬ ‭but‬ ‭since‬ ‭the‬ ‭diagonal‬ ‭line‬
‭corresponds‬ ‭to‬ ‭a‬ ‭randomly‬ ‭guessing‬ ‭classifier,‬ ‭no‬‭realistic‬‭classifier‬‭should‬‭have‬‭an‬‭AUC‬
‭below‬‭0.5.‬‭The‬‭AUC‬‭is‬‭equivalent‬‭to‬‭the‬‭probability‬‭that‬‭the‬‭classifier‬‭will‬‭give‬‭a‬‭higher‬‭score‬
‭to a randomly chosen positive instance than a randomly chosen negative instance.‬
‭An‬‭attractive‬‭property‬‭of‬‭the‬‭ROC‬‭curve‬‭and‬‭of‬‭AUC‬‭is‬‭that‬‭they‬‭are‬‭insensitive‬‭to‬‭changes‬‭in‬
‭the‬‭relative‬‭frequency‬‭of‬‭negative‬‭and‬‭positive‬‭instances‬‭in‬‭a‬‭dataset.‬‭That‬‭is‬‭the‬‭ROC‬‭curve‬
‭and‬‭its‬‭AUC‬‭will‬‭stay‬‭the‬‭same‬‭if‬‭the‬‭proportion‬‭of‬‭positive‬‭to‬‭negative‬‭instances‬‭change.‬‭This‬
‭means‬‭that‬‭they‬‭are‬‭suitable‬‭metrics‬‭for‬‭imbalanced‬‭datasets.‬‭The‬‭AUC‬‭like‬‭the‬‭ROC‬‭curve‬
‭also‬ ‭does‬ ‭not‬‭care‬‭about‬‭the‬‭absolute‬‭scores‬‭of‬‭instances‬‭but‬‭only‬‭their‬‭ranking‬‭(Fawcett‬
‭2006)‬‭.‬
‭2.3.2 AUC 0.1‬
‭In‬ ‭this‬ ‭project,‬ ‭models‬ ‭that‬ ‭can‬ ‭identify‬ ‭sequences‬ ‭of‬ ‭TCRs‬ ‭that‬ ‭can‬ ‭bind‬ ‭to‬ ‭specific‬
‭peptide:MHC‬‭molecules‬‭are‬‭developed.‬‭The‬‭identification‬‭of‬‭such‬‭TCRs‬‭can‬‭be‬‭of‬‭great‬‭use‬
‭in‬‭the‬‭development‬‭of‬‭T-cell‬‭based‬‭vaccines‬‭and‬‭in‬‭targeted‬‭immunotherapy.‬‭In‬‭these‬‭cases‬‭it‬
‭is‬‭of‬‭great‬‭interest‬‭to‬‭keep‬‭the‬‭FPR‬‭low‬‭in‬‭order‬‭to‬‭not‬‭waste‬‭resources‬‭on‬‭analysis‬‭of‬‭TCRs‬
‭19‬
==PAGE:24==
‭that‬‭in‬‭the‬‭end‬‭turn‬‭out‬‭to‬‭be‬‭false‬‭positives.‬‭And‬‭since‬‭there‬‭are‬‭many‬‭more‬‭non-binders‬
‭than binders for a given TCR it becomes even more important.‬
‭In‬‭these‬‭cases,‬‭it‬‭is‬‭thus‬‭most‬‭interesting‬‭to‬‭have‬‭good‬‭performance‬‭on‬‭the‬‭far‬‭left-hand‬‭side‬
‭of‬‭the‬‭ROC‬‭graph.‬‭When‬‭comparing‬‭two‬‭ROC‬‭curves‬‭one‬‭may‬‭be‬‭superior‬‭for‬‭low‬‭FPR‬‭but‬
‭overall‬‭worse‬‭than‬‭the‬‭other.‬‭This‬‭is‬‭not‬‭reflected‬‭in‬‭the‬‭metric‬‭AUC.‬‭Instead‬‭of‬‭integrating‬
‭under‬‭the‬‭entire‬‭ROC‬‭curve‬‭one‬‭can‬‭integrate‬‭only‬‭in‬‭the‬‭FPR‬‭interval‬‭of‬‭interest‬‭to‬‭get‬‭a‬
‭partial‬ ‭AUC.‬ ‭AUC‬ ‭0.1‬ ‭is‬ ‭defined‬ ‭as‬ ‭the‬ ‭partial‬‭AUC‬‭in‬‭the‬‭FPR‬‭interval‬‭[0,‬‭0.1]‬‭and‬‭thus‬
‭focuses on the performance for low FPR.‬
‭Since‬‭AUC‬‭0.1‬‭is‬‭a‬‭partial‬‭AUC‬‭it‬‭will‬‭no‬‭longer‬‭have‬‭a‬‭maximum‬‭of‬‭1.‬‭It‬‭can‬‭be‬‭standardized‬
‭to‬‭have‬‭the‬‭same‬‭scale‬‭as‬‭AUC‬‭using‬‭the‬‭McClish‬‭correction‬‭(McClish‬‭1989)‬‭.‬‭In‬‭the‬‭context‬
‭of‬‭this‬‭project,‬‭when‬‭mentioning‬‭AUC‬‭0.1,‬‭it‬‭specifically‬‭refers‬‭to‬‭the‬‭standardized‬‭version‬‭of‬
‭the metric achieved through the McClish correction.‬
‭2.3.3 PPV‬
‭Positive‬ ‭predictive‬ ‭value‬ ‭(PPV)‬ ‭will‬ ‭also‬ ‭be‬ ‭used‬ ‭as‬ ‭a‬ ‭performance‬ ‭metric,‬ ‭which‬ ‭for‬ ‭a‬
‭discrete‬‭classifier‬‭and‬‭set‬‭of‬‭instances‬‭is‬‭normally‬‭defined‬‭as‬‭the‬‭fraction‬‭of‬‭actual‬‭positives‬
‭correctly‬ ‭classified‬ ‭as‬ ‭positives‬ ‭(TP)‬ ‭out‬‭of‬‭all‬‭instances‬‭predicted‬‭to‬‭be‬‭positive‬‭(TP+FP)‬
‭(Herlau, Mørup, and Schmidt 2019)‬‭:‬
‭(2.17)‬
‭In‬‭this‬‭project‬‭an‬‭alternative‬‭definition‬‭of‬‭PPV‬‭will‬‭be‬‭used.‬‭In‬‭this‬‭definition‬‭the‬‭number‬‭of‬‭all‬
‭actual‬‭positive‬‭instances‬‭P‬‭in‬‭the‬‭set‬‭is‬‭counted.‬‭Then‬‭PPV‬‭is‬‭computed‬‭as‬‭the‬‭fraction‬‭of‬
‭actual positives‬
‭within the top P highest scoring instances:‬
‭(2.18)‬
‭It‬‭can‬‭however‬‭be‬‭shown‬‭that‬‭this‬‭alternative‬‭definition‬‭is‬‭equivalent‬‭to‬‭the‬‭classical‬‭definition‬
‭(equation‬‭2.17)‬‭where‬‭the‬‭threshold‬
‭for‬‭turning‬‭a‬‭scoring‬‭classifier‬‭into‬‭a‬‭discrete‬‭classifier‬
‭is found such that the number of false positives equals the number of false negatives:‬
‭(2.19)‬
‭If‬‭we‬‭set‬‭a‬‭threshold‬
‭to‬‭the‬‭minimum‬‭score‬‭of‬‭the‬‭P‬‭highest‬‭scoring‬‭instances,‬‭then‬‭the‬
‭number‬‭of‬‭top‬‭P‬‭highest‬‭scoring‬‭instances‬‭is‬‭the‬‭number‬‭of‬‭instances‬‭that‬‭are‬‭classified‬‭as‬
‭positive:‬
‭(2.20)‬
‭In‬ ‭addition‬ ‭the‬ ‭number‬ ‭of‬ ‭actual‬ ‭positives‬
‭within‬ ‭the‬ ‭set‬ ‭of‬ ‭top‬ ‭P‬ ‭highest‬ ‭scoring‬
‭instances is the number of true positives:‬
‭(2.21)‬
‭Inserting equation 2.20 and 2.21 into 2.18 we get the classical definition:‬
‭Furthermore,‬‭it‬‭holds‬‭that‬‭the‬‭number‬‭of‬‭all‬‭actual‬‭positives‬‭P‬‭is‬‭equal‬‭to‬‭the‬‭sum‬‭of‬‭TP‬‭and‬
‭FN:‬
‭(2.22)‬
‭From equation 2.20 and 2.22 we thus see that equation 2.19 holds for the selected threshold.‬
‭20‬
==PAGE:25==
‭Interestingly,‬‭if‬‭we‬‭insert‬‭equation‬‭2.21‬‭and‬‭2.22‬‭into‬‭2.18‬‭we‬‭see‬‭that‬‭PPV‬‭is‬‭equal‬‭to‬‭TPR‬
‭for the selected threshold:‬
‭(2.23)‬
‭The‬ ‭alternative‬ ‭definition‬ ‭of‬ ‭PPV‬ ‭has‬ ‭some‬ ‭useful‬ ‭properties‬ ‭for‬ ‭an‬ ‭imbalanced‬ ‭dataset‬
‭dominated‬‭by‬‭negative‬‭instances,‬‭which‬‭is‬‭what‬‭we‬‭have‬‭in‬‭this‬‭project.‬‭Here‬‭the‬‭number‬‭of‬
‭all actual negative instances N has the following relation to P:‬
‭(2.24)‬
‭Inserting‬ ‭equation‬ ‭2.19‬ ‭and‬ ‭2.24‬ ‭into‬ ‭the‬ ‭expression‬ ‭for‬ ‭FPR‬ ‭(equation‬‭2.16)‬‭we‬‭get‬‭the‬
‭following:‬
‭(2.25)‬
‭The‬‭metric‬
‭is‬‭the‬‭false‬‭negative‬‭rate‬‭(FNR)‬‭and‬‭describes‬‭the‬‭fraction‬‭of‬‭actual‬‭positives‬
‭incorrectly classified as negatives out of all actual positives.‬
‭Thus‬ ‭for‬ ‭an‬ ‭imbalanced‬ ‭dataset‬ ‭dominated‬ ‭by‬ ‭negative‬ ‭instances,‬ ‭when‬ ‭we‬ ‭evaluate‬ ‭a‬
‭scoring‬‭classifier‬‭with‬‭respect‬‭to‬‭PPV‬‭for‬‭a‬‭threshold‬‭set‬‭such‬‭that‬
‭it‬‭means‬‭that‬
‭we evaluate it for a threshold set such that FPR is kept low relative to FNR.‬
‭Since‬‭the‬‭maximum‬‭possible‬‭value‬‭for‬‭FNR‬‭is‬‭1‬‭it‬‭is‬‭observed‬‭from‬‭equation‬‭2.25‬‭that‬‭the‬
‭maximum‬‭possible‬‭value‬‭for‬‭FPR‬‭must‬‭be‬ ‭.‬‭Thus‬‭we‬‭have‬‭limited‬‭the‬‭FPR‬‭as‬‭was‬‭the‬‭case‬
‭for AUC 0.1.‬
‭2.4 Embeddings and Transformers‬
‭Amino‬‭acid‬‭sequences‬‭are‬‭typically‬‭represented‬‭using‬‭a‬‭one-letter‬‭code,‬‭where‬‭each‬‭letter‬
‭corresponds‬‭to‬‭a‬‭specific‬‭amino‬‭acid.‬‭Neural‬‭networks‬‭do‬‭however‬‭not‬‭understand‬‭letters,‬
‭but‬ ‭only‬ ‭numbers.‬ ‭A‬ ‭simple‬‭solution‬‭to‬‭this‬‭is‬‭known‬‭as‬‭one-hot‬‭encoding.‬‭This‬‭encoding‬
‭scheme‬‭has‬‭a‬‭mapping‬‭for‬‭each‬‭of‬‭the‬‭twenty‬‭different‬‭amino‬‭acids‬‭to‬‭different‬‭vectors‬‭all‬
‭with‬‭a‬‭length‬‭of‬‭twenty.‬‭The‬‭vectors‬‭are‬‭all‬‭filled‬‭with‬‭zeros‬‭except‬‭for‬‭one‬‭entry‬‭that‬‭is‬‭set‬‭to‬
‭one.‬‭The‬‭location‬‭of‬‭the‬‭one-entry‬‭differs‬‭for‬‭all‬‭of‬‭the‬‭twenty‬‭amino‬‭acids.‬‭The‬‭issue‬‭with‬
‭one-hot-encoding‬‭is‬‭that‬‭it‬‭assumes‬‭that‬‭the‬‭20‬‭amino‬‭acids‬‭are‬‭all‬‭independent‬‭from‬‭each‬
‭other,‬‭since‬‭the‬‭formed‬‭vectors‬‭are‬‭all‬‭orthogonal‬‭to‬‭one‬‭another.‬‭This‬‭is‬‭clearly‬‭wrong‬‭since‬
‭amino‬‭acid‬‭substitution‬‭can‬‭happen‬‭in‬‭proteins‬‭without‬‭altering‬‭the‬‭function‬‭of‬‭the‬‭protein.‬
‭Amino‬‭acids‬‭that‬‭can‬‭be‬‭substituted‬‭with‬‭each‬‭other‬‭should‬‭be‬‭represented‬‭by‬‭vectors‬‭close‬
‭to‬‭each‬‭other‬‭(Chollet‬‭2018)‬‭.‬‭A‬‭solution‬‭is‬‭to‬‭use‬‭a‬‭BLOSUM‬‭matrix‬‭(Henikoff‬‭and‬‭Henikoff‬
‭1992)‬ ‭to‬ ‭encode‬ ‭amino‬ ‭acid‬ ‭sequences.‬ ‭In‬ ‭this‬ ‭encoding‬ ‭scheme‬ ‭each‬ ‭amino‬ ‭acid‬ ‭is‬
‭mapped‬‭to‬‭a‬‭vector‬‭again‬‭of‬‭length‬‭twenty,‬‭but‬‭this‬‭time‬‭the‬‭different‬‭entries‬‭of‬‭the‬‭vector‬
‭represent‬‭how‬‭often‬‭the‬‭amino‬‭acid‬‭in‬‭question‬‭has‬‭been‬‭observed‬‭to‬‭be‬‭substituted‬‭with‬‭one‬
‭of‬‭the‬‭other‬‭20‬‭amino‬‭acids‬‭over‬‭evolutionary‬‭time.‬‭It‬‭has‬‭been‬‭shown‬‭that‬‭the‬‭geometrical‬
‭proximity‬‭of‬‭these‬‭vectors‬‭relate‬‭to‬‭the‬‭chemical‬‭similarity‬‭of‬‭the‬‭amino‬‭acids‬‭they‬‭represent‬
‭(Li and Koehl 2014)‬‭.‬
‭Even‬‭though‬‭BLOSUM‬‭encoding‬‭captures‬‭some‬‭features‬‭of‬‭the‬‭amino‬‭acids‬‭by‬‭placing‬‭their‬
‭vector‬ ‭representations‬ ‭accordingly‬ ‭in‬ ‭geometric‬ ‭space,‬ ‭it‬ ‭might‬ ‭not‬ ‭capture‬ ‭all‬ ‭relevant‬
‭21‬
==PAGE:26==
‭features‬‭for‬‭a‬‭given‬‭problem.‬‭Embeddings‬‭of‬‭amino‬‭acid‬‭sequences‬‭can‬‭instead‬‭be‬‭made.‬
‭Here‬ ‭the‬ ‭amino‬ ‭acids‬ ‭in‬ ‭a‬ ‭sequence‬ ‭are‬ ‭represented‬ ‭by‬ ‭floating-point‬ ‭vectors‬ ‭that‬ ‭are‬
‭densely‬‭packed‬‭with‬‭information‬‭learned‬‭from‬‭data,‬‭potentially‬‭containing‬‭more‬‭features‬‭that‬
‭are‬‭important‬‭for‬‭the‬‭given‬‭problem.‬‭Amino‬‭acid‬‭embeddings‬‭are‬‭structured‬‭representations‬
‭that‬‭strive‬‭to‬‭place‬‭similar‬‭amino‬‭acids‬‭close‬‭to‬‭each‬‭other‬‭in‬‭geometrical‬‭space,‬‭as‬‭was‬‭also‬
‭the‬ ‭case‬ ‭for‬ ‭the‬ ‭BLOSUM‬‭encodings.‬ ‭Machine‬‭learning‬‭architectures‬‭called‬‭transformers‬
‭can‬‭be‬‭used‬‭to‬‭learn‬‭these‬‭embeddings.‬‭What‬‭is‬‭special‬‭about‬‭transformers‬‭is‬‭that‬‭besides‬
‭having‬‭the‬‭ability‬‭to‬‭produce‬‭embeddings‬‭where‬‭similar‬‭amino‬‭acids‬‭are‬‭placed‬‭close‬‭to‬‭each‬
‭other‬‭they‬‭also‬‭produce‬‭embeddings‬‭that‬‭contain‬‭contextual‬‭and‬‭positional‬‭information.‬‭The‬
‭contextual‬‭information‬‭means‬‭that‬‭a‬‭given‬‭amino‬‭acid,‬‭say‬‭N‬‭(asparagine)‬‭gets‬‭a‬‭different‬
‭vector‬‭representation‬‭depending‬‭on‬‭the‬‭sequence‬‭it‬‭is‬‭located‬‭in‬‭-‬‭that‬‭is‬‭the‬‭context‬‭of‬‭the‬
‭other‬‭amino‬‭acids‬‭in‬‭the‬‭sequence‬‭plays‬‭a‬‭role‬‭for‬‭its‬‭embedding.‬‭The‬‭positional‬‭information‬
‭means‬ ‭that‬ ‭the‬ ‭position‬ ‭of‬ ‭N‬ ‭in‬ ‭a‬ ‭sequence‬ ‭also‬‭is‬‭captured‬‭in‬‭its‬‭vector‬‭representation.‬
‭BLOSUM‬‭and‬‭one-hot-encodings‬‭both‬‭lack‬‭contextual‬‭and‬‭positional‬‭information,‬‭which‬‭may‬
‭compromise model performance.‬
‭In‬‭the‬‭case‬‭where‬‭one's‬‭training‬‭dataset‬‭is‬‭limited‬‭in‬‭size,‬‭pretrained‬‭transformers‬‭that‬‭have‬
‭learned‬ ‭the‬ ‭general‬ ‭aspects‬ ‭of‬ ‭protein‬ ‭sequences‬ ‭from‬ ‭huge‬ ‭datasets‬ ‭can‬ ‭be‬ ‭used‬ ‭to‬
‭generate‬ ‭embeddings‬ ‭that‬ ‭are‬ ‭hopefully‬‭also‬‭appropriate‬‭for‬‭the‬‭specific‬‭problem‬‭at‬‭hand‬
‭(Chollet 2018)‬‭. This is what will be investigated‬‭in this project.‬
‭More‬‭specifically‬‭embeddings‬‭from‬‭the‬‭following‬‭transformers‬‭will‬‭be‬‭investigated:‬‭ESM-1b‬
‭(Rives‬‭et‬‭al.‬‭2021)‬‭,‬‭ProtT5-XL-U50‬‭(Elnaggar‬‭et‬‭al.‬‭2022)‬‭and‬‭the‬‭variant‬‭of‬‭ESM-2‬‭with‬‭650‬
‭million parameters‬‭(Z. Lin et al. 2023)‬‭.‬
‭The‬ ‭ESM-1b‬ ‭transformer‬ ‭is‬ ‭trained‬ ‭on‬ ‭a‬ ‭UniRef50‬ ‭2018-03‬ ‭dataset‬ ‭from‬ ‭UniProt‬ ‭(The‬
‭UniProt‬‭Consortium‬‭et‬‭al.‬‭2023)‬‭containing‬‭~30‬‭million‬‭protein‬‭sequences.‬‭It‬‭has‬‭33‬‭layers,‬
‭~650‬ ‭million‬ ‭parameters‬ ‭and‬ ‭produces‬‭embeddings‬‭with‬‭an‬‭embedding‬‭dimension‬‭size‬‭of‬
‭1280.‬
‭The‬‭ProtT5-XL-U50‬‭transformer‬‭is‬‭trained‬‭on‬‭a‬‭dataset‬‭that‬‭combines‬‭a‬‭UniRef50‬‭dataset‬‭of‬
‭~45‬ ‭million‬ ‭protein‬ ‭sequences‬ ‭with‬ ‭a‬ ‭dataset‬ ‭from‬‭BSD‬‭of‬‭~2.1‬‭billion‬‭protein‬‭sequences‬
‭(Jumper‬‭et‬‭al.‬‭2021)‬‭.‬‭It‬‭has‬‭24‬‭layers,‬‭~3‬‭billion‬‭parameters‬‭and‬‭produces‬‭embeddings‬‭with‬
‭an embedding dimension size of 1024.‬
‭Finally,‬‭the‬‭ESM-2‬‭transformer‬‭variant‬‭with‬‭650‬‭million‬‭parameters‬‭is‬‭trained‬‭on‬‭~65‬‭million‬
‭protein‬‭sequences‬‭sampled‬‭from‬‭a‬‭dataset‬‭that‬‭combines‬‭a‬‭UniRef50‬‭dataset‬‭of‬‭~43‬‭million‬
‭protein‬ ‭sequences‬ ‭with‬ ‭a‬ ‭UniRef90‬ ‭dataset‬ ‭of‬ ‭~138‬ ‭million‬ ‭protein‬ ‭sequences.‬ ‭It‬‭has‬‭33‬
‭layers and produces embeddings with an embedding dimension size of 1280.‬
‭All‬ ‭three‬ ‭transformer‬ ‭models‬ ‭were‬ ‭trained‬ ‭in‬ ‭a‬ ‭self-supervised‬ ‭manner‬ ‭where‬ ‭each‬‭input‬
‭sequence‬ ‭was‬ ‭corrupted‬ ‭by‬ ‭replacing‬ ‭a‬ ‭fraction‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭amino‬ ‭acids‬ ‭with‬ ‭a‬ ‭special‬ ‭mask‬
‭token.‬‭The‬‭transformers‬‭were‬‭then‬‭trained‬‭to‬‭predict‬‭the‬‭missing‬‭tokens‬‭from‬‭the‬‭corrupted‬
‭sequence.‬
‭22‬
==PAGE:27==
‭2.5 Convolutional Neural Networks‬
‭A‬‭convolutional‬‭neural‬‭network‬‭(CNN)‬‭is‬‭a‬‭specialized‬‭FFN‬‭used‬‭for‬‭processing‬‭data‬‭where‬
‭we‬‭can‬‭assume‬‭that‬‭the‬‭spatial‬‭structure‬‭of‬‭the‬‭data‬‭is‬‭important‬‭for‬‭getting‬‭good‬‭predictions‬
‭out‬‭of‬‭the‬‭neural‬‭network.‬‭The‬‭input‬‭data‬‭must‬‭have‬‭a‬‭grid-like‬‭topology.‬‭Examples‬‭include‬
‭image‬ ‭data‬ ‭with‬ ‭a‬ ‭two-dimensional‬ ‭grid‬ ‭of‬ ‭pixels‬ ‭or‬ ‭amino‬ ‭acid‬ ‭sequences‬ ‭with‬ ‭a‬
‭one-dimensional‬ ‭grid‬ ‭of‬ ‭amino‬ ‭acids.‬‭Assumptions‬‭about‬‭the‬‭spatial‬‭structure‬‭of‬‭the‬‭input‬
‭data‬ ‭are‬ ‭built‬ ‭into‬‭the‬‭architecture‬‭of‬‭CNNs,‬‭which‬‭makes‬‭it‬‭easier‬‭for‬‭them‬‭to‬‭learn‬‭from‬
‭spatially‬‭structured‬‭data‬‭than‬‭standard‬‭FNNs.‬‭Specifically,‬‭three‬‭basic‬‭mechanisms‬‭are‬‭used‬
‭in‬‭the‬‭architecture‬‭of‬‭CNNs:‬‭local‬‭receptive‬‭field‬‭,‬‭shared‬‭weights‬‭and‬‭pooling‬‭.‬‭Let‬‭us‬‭look‬‭at‬
‭each of them in turn, starting with the local receptive field.‬
‭To‬ ‭understand‬ ‭the‬ ‭mechanisms‬ ‭we‬ ‭will‬ ‭look‬‭at‬‭the‬‭case‬‭where‬‭an‬‭embedded‬‭amino‬‭acid‬
‭sequence‬‭is‬‭fed‬‭to‬‭a‬‭CNN,‬‭where‬‭the‬‭first‬‭hidden‬‭layer‬‭is‬‭a‬‭convolutional‬‭layer.‬‭The‬‭amino‬
‭acid‬ ‭sequence‬ ‭has‬ ‭one‬ ‭spatial‬ ‭dimension‬ ‭(the‬ ‭amino‬ ‭acid‬ ‭dimension)‬ ‭and‬ ‭one‬ ‭feature‬
‭dimension‬ ‭(the‬ ‭embedding‬ ‭dimension).‬ ‭Each‬ ‭one-dimensional‬ ‭slice‬ ‭over‬ ‭the‬ ‭feature‬
‭dimension‬‭is‬‭said‬‭to‬‭be‬‭fed‬‭as‬‭different‬‭channels‬‭to‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭layer.‬‭In‬‭FNNs‬‭depicted‬
‭earlier‬‭(fig.‬‭2.1)‬‭the‬‭neurons‬‭of‬‭a‬‭layer‬‭were‬‭placed‬‭in‬‭a‬‭line.‬‭In‬‭our‬‭case‬‭where‬‭an‬‭input‬‭with‬
‭one‬‭spatial‬‭dimension‬‭and‬‭one‬‭feature‬‭dimension‬‭is‬‭fed‬‭into‬‭the‬‭input‬‭layer‬‭of‬‭a‬‭CNN,‬‭it‬‭is‬
‭instead‬ ‭helpful‬ ‭to‬ ‭think‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭neurons‬ ‭in‬ ‭the‬ ‭input‬ ‭layer‬ ‭as‬ ‭being‬ ‭organized‬ ‭in‬ ‭a‬
‭two-dimensional‬ ‭grid‬ ‭so‬ ‭it‬ ‭matches‬ ‭the‬ ‭input.‬ ‭The‬ ‭neurons‬ ‭in‬ ‭the‬ ‭convolutional‬ ‭layer‬‭are‬
‭however‬‭placed‬‭in‬‭a‬‭line,‬‭but‬‭we‬‭will‬‭see‬‭later‬‭that‬‭it‬‭makes‬‭sense‬‭to‬‭stack‬‭multiple‬‭of‬‭such‬
‭lines, so that the convolutional layer also becomes a two dimensional grid.‬
‭Each‬‭hidden‬‭neuron‬‭of‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭layer‬‭is‬‭unlike‬‭a‬‭dense‬‭layer‬‭in‬‭a‬‭standard‬‭FNN‬‭not‬
‭connected‬‭to‬‭every‬‭neuron‬‭of‬‭the‬‭input‬‭layer.‬‭It‬‭is‬‭instead‬‭sparsely‬‭connected‬‭to‬‭only‬‭a‬‭small‬
‭region‬‭of‬‭input‬‭neurons.‬‭This‬‭is‬‭the‬‭hidden‬‭neuron's‬‭local‬‭receptive‬‭field.‬‭To‬‭be‬‭more‬‭precise‬
‭the‬ ‭connections‬ ‭of‬ ‭a‬ ‭convolutional‬ ‭neuron‬ ‭to‬ ‭the‬ ‭input‬‭layer‬‭are‬‭local‬‭in‬‭one-dimensional‬
‭space‬‭(the‬‭spatial‬‭dimension‬‭of‬‭the‬‭image)‬‭but‬‭full‬‭along‬‭the‬‭entire‬‭feature/depth‬‭dimension.‬
‭Each‬‭connection‬‭to‬‭a‬‭convolutional‬‭neuron‬‭has‬‭an‬‭associated‬‭weight‬‭and‬‭each‬‭convolutional‬
‭neuron‬ ‭also‬ ‭has‬ ‭a‬ ‭bias,‬ ‭just‬ ‭as‬ ‭we‬ ‭saw‬ ‭for‬ ‭a‬ ‭standard‬ ‭FNN.‬ ‭We‬ ‭can‬ ‭think‬ ‭of‬ ‭each‬
‭convolutional‬‭neuron‬‭as‬‭learning‬‭to‬‭analyze‬‭its‬‭particular‬‭local‬‭receptive‬‭field‬‭and‬‭learning‬‭to‬
‭extract‬ ‭local‬ ‭features‬ ‭from‬ ‭the‬ ‭input.‬ ‭This‬ ‭makes‬ ‭sense‬‭for‬‭amino‬‭acid‬‭sequences‬‭where‬
‭nearby‬‭amino‬‭acids‬‭are‬‭normally‬‭more‬‭strongly‬‭correlated‬‭than‬‭distant‬‭amino‬‭acids,‬‭meaning‬
‭we can learn a local pattern also called a local feature.‬
‭The‬‭local‬‭receptive‬‭field‬‭is‬‭slided‬‭across‬‭the‬‭entire‬‭spatial‬‭space‬‭of‬‭the‬‭amino‬‭acid‬‭sequence.‬
‭For‬‭each‬‭local‬‭receptive‬‭field,‬‭there‬‭is‬‭a‬‭different‬‭neuron‬‭in‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭layer.‬‭This‬‭forms‬
‭a‬‭one-dimensional‬‭grid‬‭of‬‭neurons‬‭in‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭layer‬‭also‬‭known‬‭as‬‭a‬‭feature‬‭map,‬
‭since‬‭it‬‭detects‬‭local‬‭features‬‭from‬‭the‬‭input‬‭(see‬‭fig‬‭2.7a).‬‭The‬‭amount‬‭of‬‭neurons‬‭the‬‭local‬
‭receptive‬‭field‬‭is‬‭moved‬‭at‬‭a‬‭time‬‭is‬‭known‬‭as‬‭the‬‭stride‬‭length.‬‭In‬‭fig‬‭2.7b‬‭a‬‭stride‬‭length‬‭of‬
‭one is illustrated.‬
‭23‬
==PAGE:28==
‭Fig.‬ ‭2.7.‬ ‭Illustration‬ ‭of‬ ‭local‬ ‭receptive‬ ‭fields.‬ ‭It‬ ‭is‬‭shown‬‭how‬‭neurons‬‭in‬‭a‬‭feature‬‭map‬‭of‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬
‭layer‬‭are‬‭connected‬‭to‬‭neurons‬‭in‬‭the‬‭input‬‭layer.‬‭Neurons‬‭are‬‭depicted‬‭as‬‭nodes.‬‭One-dimensional‬‭slices‬‭across‬
‭the‬ ‭feature‬ ‭dimension‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭input‬ ‭layer‬ ‭are‬ ‭illustrated‬ ‭as‬‭filled‬‭rectangles‬‭stacked‬‭on‬‭top‬‭of‬‭each‬‭other.‬‭In‬‭the‬
‭convolutional‬ ‭layer‬‭there‬‭is‬‭only‬‭one‬‭rectangle‬‭corresponding‬‭to‬‭a‬‭feature‬‭map.‬‭The‬‭lines‬‭connected‬‭to‬‭a‬‭single‬
‭neuron‬‭of‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭layer‬‭illustrates‬‭the‬‭local‬‭receptive‬‭field‬‭for‬‭that‬‭neuron.‬‭(a)‬‭Local‬‭receptive‬‭field‬‭for‬
‭first‬ ‭convolutional‬ ‭neuron.‬ ‭The‬ ‭local‬‭receptive‬‭field‬‭is‬‭illustrated‬‭for‬‭the‬‭first‬‭neuron‬‭in‬‭the‬‭feature‬‭map‬‭of‬‭the‬
‭convolutional‬ ‭layer.‬ ‭(b)‬ ‭Local‬ ‭receptive‬ ‭field‬ ‭for‬ ‭second‬ ‭convolutional‬ ‭neuron.‬ ‭The‬ ‭local‬ ‭receptive‬ ‭field‬ ‭is‬
‭illustrated‬ ‭for‬ ‭the‬ ‭second‬ ‭neuron‬ ‭in‬ ‭the‬ ‭feature‬ ‭map‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭convolutional‬ ‭layer.‬ ‭It‬ ‭is‬ ‭observed‬ ‭how‬ ‭the‬ ‭local‬
‭receptive field is slided one neuron down compared to (a). This corresponds to a stride length of one.‬
‭Sliding‬‭a‬‭local‬‭receptive‬‭field‬‭over‬‭the‬‭input‬‭causes‬‭the‬‭dimensions‬‭of‬‭the‬‭feature‬‭map‬‭in‬‭the‬
‭convolutional‬‭layer‬‭to‬‭be‬‭smaller‬‭than‬‭the‬‭spatial‬‭dimensions‬‭of‬‭the‬‭input‬‭layer‬‭(observe‬‭in‬‭fig‬
‭2.7‬‭how‬‭there‬‭are‬‭only‬‭6‬‭convolutional‬‭neurons‬‭in‬‭the‬‭feature‬‭map‬‭but‬‭10‬‭neurons‬‭in‬‭a‬‭slice‬
‭of‬‭the‬‭input‬‭layer).‬‭Padding‬‭(of‬‭usually‬‭zeros)‬‭can‬‭be‬‭added‬‭around‬‭the‬‭spatial‬‭borders‬‭of‬‭the‬
‭input to prevent “washing away” information at the spatial borders.‬
‭Let‬‭us‬‭now‬‭look‬‭at‬‭the‬‭concept‬‭of‬‭shared‬‭weights.‬‭As‬‭already‬‭mentioned‬‭each‬‭convolutional‬
‭neuron‬ ‭has‬ ‭a‬‭bias‬‭and‬‭weights‬‭associated‬‭with‬‭each‬‭of‬‭its‬‭input‬‭connections.‬‭But‬‭what‬‭is‬
‭special‬‭about‬‭convolutional‬‭layers‬‭compared‬‭to‬‭dense‬‭layers‬‭is‬‭that‬‭the‬‭neurons‬‭in‬‭a‬‭feature‬
‭map‬‭of‬‭a‬‭convolutional‬‭layer‬‭have‬‭a‬‭shared‬‭set‬‭(called‬‭a‬‭filter)‬‭consisting‬‭of‬‭input‬‭weights‬‭and‬
‭a‬‭bias.‬‭This‬‭means‬‭for‬‭a‬‭local‬‭receptive‬‭field‬‭of‬‭spatial‬‭size‬
‭and‬‭with‬‭depth‬‭(or‬‭number‬‭of‬
‭channels)‬
‭that the activation of the‬ ‭-th neuron in a feature map is given‬‭by:‬
‭(2.26)‬
‭Where‬
‭is‬‭an‬‭activation‬‭function,‬
‭is‬‭the‬‭shared‬‭bias,‬
‭is‬‭the‬
‭-th‬‭entry‬‭in‬‭a‬
‭matrix‬
‭of‬‭shared‬‭weights‬‭and‬‭finally‬
‭denotes‬‭the‬‭input‬‭activation‬‭at‬‭position‬
‭.‬
‭The‬‭operation‬
‭is‬‭the‬‭one-dimensional‬‭cross-correlation‬‭between‬‭slice‬
‭of‬
‭(called‬ ‭a‬ ‭kernel)‬ ‭and‬ ‭slice‬
‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭input‬ ‭activations.‬ ‭In‬ ‭machine‬ ‭learning‬ ‭literature‬ ‭the‬
‭operation‬ ‭is‬ ‭often‬ ‭wrongly‬ ‭called‬ ‭a‬ ‭convolution,‬ ‭hence‬ ‭the‬ ‭name‬ ‭convolutional‬ ‭neural‬
‭networks.‬‭Since‬‭the‬‭cross-correlation‬‭happens‬‭over‬‭one‬‭dimension‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭layer‬‭is‬
‭said to be a one-dimensional convolutional layer.‬
‭Since‬‭the‬‭set‬‭consisting‬‭of‬‭weights‬‭and‬‭a‬‭bias‬‭is‬‭shared‬‭among‬‭neurons‬‭in‬‭a‬‭feature‬‭map,‬‭all‬
‭neurons‬‭of‬‭the‬‭feature‬‭map‬‭learn‬‭to‬‭detect‬‭the‬‭same‬‭feature‬‭just‬‭at‬‭different‬‭spatial‬‭locations‬
‭of‬‭the‬‭amino‬‭acid‬‭sequence.‬‭This‬‭makes‬‭sense,‬‭since‬‭it‬‭can‬‭be‬‭assumed‬‭that‬‭a‬‭given‬‭basic‬
‭pattern can appear in multiple places of amino acid sequences.‬
‭24‬
==PAGE:29==
‭To‬ ‭be‬ ‭able‬ ‭to‬ ‭learn‬ ‭more‬ ‭than‬‭one‬‭type‬‭of‬‭local‬‭feature‬‭from‬‭the‬‭amino‬‭acid‬‭sequence‬‭a‬
‭depth‬‭dimension‬‭is‬‭added‬‭to‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭layer‬‭by‬‭stacking‬‭feature‬‭maps,‬‭each‬‭having‬
‭their‬‭own‬‭filter.‬‭Each‬‭feature‬‭map‬‭learns‬‭to‬‭detect‬‭a‬‭different‬‭feature,‬‭with‬‭each‬‭feature‬‭being‬
‭detectable‬ ‭across‬ ‭the‬ ‭entire‬ ‭amino‬ ‭acid‬ ‭sequence‬ ‭(Nielsen‬ ‭2015;‬ ‭Karpathy‬ ‭2021;‬
‭Goodfellow, Bengio, and Courville 2016; Bishop 2006)‬‭.‬
‭Let‬‭us‬‭now‬‭look‬‭at‬‭the‬‭final‬‭mechanism‬‭of‬‭CNNs,‬‭namely‬‭pooling.‬‭A‬‭CNN‬‭normally‬‭contains‬
‭pooling‬ ‭layers‬ ‭in‬ ‭addition‬ ‭to‬ ‭the‬ ‭convolutional‬ ‭layers.‬ ‭They‬ ‭are‬ ‭usually‬ ‭used‬ ‭right‬ ‭after‬
‭convolutional‬ ‭layers.‬ ‭They‬ ‭work‬ ‭by‬ ‭compressing‬ ‭the‬ ‭information‬ ‭in‬ ‭the‬ ‭output‬ ‭from‬
‭convolutional‬ ‭layers,‬ ‭operating‬ ‭independently‬ ‭on‬‭each‬‭feature‬‭map.‬‭More‬‭specifically‬‭they‬
‭take‬‭each‬‭feature‬‭map‬‭output‬‭from‬‭a‬‭convolutional‬‭layer‬‭and‬‭condense‬‭it‬‭into‬‭a‬‭smaller‬‭and‬
‭simpler‬ ‭representation.‬ ‭In‬ ‭our‬ ‭case‬ ‭where‬ ‭the‬ ‭input‬ ‭has‬ ‭one‬ ‭spatial‬ ‭dimension‬ ‭and‬ ‭one‬
‭feature‬ ‭dimension,‬ ‭the‬ ‭neurons‬‭of‬‭pooling‬‭layers‬‭are‬‭placed‬‭in‬‭two-dimensional‬‭grids‬‭with‬
‭channels‬ ‭corresponding‬ ‭to‬ ‭the‬ ‭feature‬ ‭maps‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭input.‬ ‭Each‬ ‭neuron‬ ‭in‬ ‭the‬
‭one-dimensional‬ ‭grid‬ ‭of‬ ‭a‬ ‭channel‬ ‭is‬ ‭connected‬ ‭to‬ ‭neurons‬ ‭in‬ ‭a‬ ‭small‬ ‭region‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬
‭corresponding‬ ‭feature‬‭map,‬‭summarizing‬‭the‬‭output‬‭from‬‭the‬‭region.‬‭One‬‭commonly‬‭used‬
‭type‬‭of‬‭pooling‬‭is‬‭max-pooling,‬‭where‬‭a‬‭pooling‬‭neuron‬‭outputs‬‭the‬‭maximum‬‭value‬‭found‬‭in‬
‭the‬‭output‬‭of‬‭the‬‭small‬‭region‬‭(Nielsen‬‭2015)‬‭.‬‭In‬‭global‬‭max-pooling‬‭there‬‭is‬‭only‬‭one‬‭neuron‬
‭per‬‭feature‬‭map,‬‭connected‬‭to‬‭all‬‭neurons‬‭of‬‭the‬‭feature‬‭map‬‭and‬‭outputting‬‭the‬‭maximum‬
‭value found in the entire feature map output‬‭(M. Lin,‬‭Chen, and Yan 2013)‬‭.‬
‭Max-pooling‬‭throws‬‭away‬‭the‬‭exact‬‭position‬‭of‬‭a‬‭found‬‭feature‬‭in‬‭the‬‭output‬‭of‬‭a‬‭feature‬‭map‬
‭and‬‭only‬‭keeps‬‭its‬‭rough‬‭position.‬‭This‬‭helps‬‭to‬‭make‬‭the‬‭learned‬‭representation‬‭of‬‭the‬‭input‬
‭to‬ ‭the‬ ‭CNN‬ ‭approximately‬ ‭invariant‬ ‭to‬ ‭small‬ ‭translations‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭input,‬ ‭meaning‬‭that‬‭if‬‭we‬
‭translate‬ ‭the‬ ‭input‬ ‭by‬ ‭a‬ ‭small‬ ‭amount,‬ ‭the‬ ‭values‬ ‭of‬ ‭most‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭pooled‬ ‭outputs‬ ‭do‬ ‭not‬
‭change‬‭(Nielsen‬‭2015;‬‭Goodfellow,‬‭Bengio,‬‭and‬‭Courville‬‭2016)‬‭.‬‭Global‬‭max-pooling‬‭throws‬
‭the‬‭position‬‭of‬‭a‬‭found‬‭feature‬‭in‬‭the‬‭output‬‭of‬‭a‬‭feature‬‭map‬‭away‬‭entirely.‬‭It‬‭is‬‭thus‬‭used‬‭to‬
‭tell the presence or absence of a feature for each feature map‬‭(M. Lin, Chen, and Yan 2013)‬‭.‬
‭It‬‭is‬‭common‬‭to‬‭transition‬‭into‬‭dense‬‭layers‬‭after‬‭one‬‭or‬‭multiple‬‭pairs‬‭of‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬
‭and‬‭pooling‬‭layer.‬‭We‬‭can‬‭think‬‭of‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭and‬‭pooling‬‭layers‬‭as‬‭learning‬‭the‬‭spatial‬
‭structure‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭input,‬ ‭while‬ ‭the‬ ‭dense‬ ‭layers‬ ‭learn‬ ‭on‬ ‭a‬ ‭more‬ ‭abstract‬ ‭level,‬ ‭integrating‬
‭information from across the entire input in order to be able to predict correctly‬‭(Nielsen 2015)‬‭.‬
‭25‬
==PAGE:30==
‭3 Methods‬
‭3.1 Data‬
‭The‬ ‭dataset‬ ‭used‬ ‭for‬ ‭model‬ ‭development‬ ‭had‬‭already‬‭been‬‭prepared‬‭by‬‭colleagues.‬‭The‬
‭steps‬‭involved‬‭in‬‭preparation‬‭of‬‭the‬‭dataset‬‭will‬‭be‬‭summarized‬‭in‬‭the‬‭following‬‭sections,‬‭but‬
‭can be found described in more detail in‬‭(Jensen and Nielsen 2023)‬‭.‬
‭3.1.1 Raw Data‬
‭Data‬‭was‬‭acquired‬‭from‬‭the‬‭Immune‬‭Epitope‬‭Database‬‭(IEDB)‬‭(Vita‬‭et‬‭al.‬‭2019)‬‭and‬‭VDJdb‬
‭(Goncharov‬‭et‬‭al.‬‭2022)‬‭on‬‭August‬‭23rd‬‭and‬‭24th,‬‭2022,‬‭respectively.‬‭In‬‭addition,‬‭a‬‭dataset‬
‭from‬‭a‬‭10x‬‭sequencing‬‭study‬‭(10x‬‭Genomics‬‭2020)‬‭was‬‭included,‬‭which‬‭had‬‭been‬‭denoised‬
‭with‬‭a‬‭tool‬‭called‬‭ITRAP‬‭(Povlsen‬‭et‬‭al.‬‭2023)‬‭.‬‭All‬‭data‬‭consists‬‭of‬‭only‬‭human‬‭T-cell‬‭assays‬
‭that‬‭have‬‭a‬‭positive‬‭result‬‭for‬‭the‬‭binding‬‭of‬‭TCR‬‭to‬‭an‬‭MHC‬‭I:peptide‬‭complex.‬‭Furthermore,‬
‭the‬‭data‬‭is‬‭for‬‭paired‬‭α-/β-chains‬‭only.‬‭From‬‭IEDB,‬‭21,825‬‭observations‬‭across‬‭631‬‭peptides‬
‭were collected and from VDJdb 27,005 observations across 898 peptides were acquired.‬
‭3.1.2 Data Preparation‬
‭Since‬‭the‬‭raw‬‭data‬‭contained‬‭incomplete‬‭TCR‬‭sequences,‬‭the‬‭software‬‭stitchr‬‭(Heather‬‭et‬‭al.‬
‭2022)‬‭was‬‭used‬‭to‬‭generate‬‭full‬‭TCR‬‭sequences.‬‭Afterwards,‬‭the‬‭CDR1,‬‭CDR2‬‭and‬‭CDR3‬
‭amino‬‭acid‬‭sequences‬‭were‬‭annotated‬‭with‬‭the‬‭use‬‭of‬‭the‬‭software‬‭ANARCI‬‭(Dunbar‬‭and‬
‭Deane 2016)‬‭.‬
‭The‬‭CDR-annotated‬‭data‬‭was‬‭redundancy‬‭reduced‬‭to‬‭improve‬‭the‬‭generalizability‬‭of‬‭models‬
‭later‬‭developed‬‭in‬‭the‬‭project.‬‭The‬‭redundancy‬‭reduction‬‭was‬‭done‬‭based‬‭on‬‭the‬‭similarity‬‭of‬
‭pairs‬‭of‬‭CDR3α‬‭and‬‭CDR3β‬‭sequences‬‭using‬‭the‬‭Hobohm‬‭1‬‭algorithm‬‭(Hobohm‬‭et‬‭al.‬‭1992)‬‭.‬
‭The‬ ‭similarity‬ ‭between‬ ‭two‬ ‭pairs‬ ‭was‬ ‭calculated‬ ‭using‬ ‭the‬ ‭BLOSUM62‬ ‭encoded‬ ‭kernel‬
‭similarity‬ ‭measure‬ ‭as‬ ‭defined‬ ‭in‬ ‭(Shen‬ ‭et‬ ‭al.‬ ‭2012)‬‭and‬‭as‬‭a‬‭sum‬‭of‬‭similarity‬‭scores‬‭for‬
‭CDR3α and CDR3β.‬
‭To‬‭keep‬‭as‬‭many‬‭unique‬‭peptides‬‭in‬‭the‬‭dataset‬‭as‬‭possible,‬‭the‬‭CDR-annotated‬‭data‬‭was‬
‭first‬‭redundancy‬‭reduced‬‭separately‬‭for‬‭sets‬‭of‬‭TCRs‬‭belonging‬‭to‬‭each‬‭unique‬‭peptide‬‭using‬
‭a‬‭similarity‬‭threshold‬‭of‬‭0.95.‬‭Afterwards,‬‭only‬‭peptides‬‭with‬‭at‬‭least‬‭30‬‭TCRs‬‭were‬‭kept‬‭in‬
‭order‬‭to‬‭have‬‭enough‬‭information‬‭per‬‭peptide‬‭for‬‭a‬‭model‬‭to‬‭learn‬‭the‬‭rules‬‭of‬‭binding‬‭of‬‭a‬
‭given‬‭peptide.‬‭In‬‭a‬‭second‬‭step‬‭redundancy‬‭reduction‬‭was‬‭carried‬‭out‬‭across‬‭all‬‭remaining‬
‭TCRs‬‭to‬‭catch‬‭similar‬‭observations‬‭between‬‭sets,‬‭again‬‭using‬‭a‬‭similarity‬‭threshold‬‭of‬‭0.95.‬
‭Here‬‭the‬‭input‬‭list‬‭to‬‭the‬‭Hobohm‬‭1‬‭algorithm‬‭was‬‭sorted‬‭by‬‭peptide‬‭according‬‭to‬‭its‬‭TCR‬
‭count‬ ‭in‬ ‭descending‬ ‭order‬ ‭to‬ ‭minimize‬ ‭the‬ ‭removal‬ ‭of‬ ‭observations‬‭for‬‭peptides‬‭with‬‭few‬
‭observations. The resulting dataset contained 6,353 observations across 26 peptides.‬
‭The‬ ‭data‬ ‭was‬ ‭then‬ ‭randomly‬ ‭split‬ ‭into‬ ‭five‬ ‭approximately‬‭equally‬‭sized‬‭partitions,‬‭so‬‭that‬
‭cross-validation could later be applied to the dataset under the model development phase.‬
‭Within‬ ‭each‬ ‭partition,‬ ‭negative‬ ‭binders‬ ‭were‬ ‭generated‬‭in‬‭a‬‭1:5‬‭ratio‬‭(five‬‭times‬‭as‬‭many‬
‭negatives‬‭as‬‭positives)‬‭by‬‭swapping‬‭TCRs‬‭for‬‭a‬‭given‬‭peptide‬‭with‬‭TCRs‬‭binding‬‭to‬‭other‬
‭non-similar‬ ‭peptides.‬ ‭Here,‬ ‭a‬ ‭non-similar‬ ‭peptide‬ ‭was‬ ‭defined‬ ‭as‬ ‭one‬ ‭which‬ ‭had‬ ‭a‬
‭Levenshtein‬ ‭distance‬ ‭of‬ ‭at‬‭least‬‭3,‬‭practically‬‭meaning‬‭that‬‭it‬‭differed‬‭by‬‭at‬‭least‬‭3‬‭amino‬
‭26‬
==PAGE:31==
‭acids.‬‭The‬‭1:5‬‭ratio‬‭was‬‭achieved‬‭for‬‭all‬‭peptides‬‭except‬‭the‬‭GILGFVFTL‬‭peptide‬‭where‬‭a‬
‭~1:4.6 ratio was achieved instead (due to the high number of positive TCRs for this peptide).‬
‭3.2 Embeddings‬
‭TCR‬‭and‬‭peptide‬‭amino‬‭acid‬‭sequences‬‭were‬‭embedded‬‭to‬‭get‬‭representations‬‭suitable‬‭for‬
‭the‬ ‭neural‬ ‭network‬ ‭training.‬ ‭Embeddings‬ ‭were‬ ‭made‬ ‭for‬ ‭two‬ ‭different‬ ‭neural‬ ‭network‬
‭architecture‬‭types‬‭-‬‭a‬‭CNN‬‭and‬‭a‬‭FNN.‬‭Creation‬‭of‬‭the‬‭embeddings‬‭for‬‭the‬‭two‬‭architecture‬
‭types will now be described.‬
‭3.2.1 CNN Embeddings‬
‭In‬ ‭one‬ ‭approach,‬ ‭peptide‬ ‭sequences‬ ‭and‬ ‭CDR1α,‬ ‭CDR2α,‬ ‭CDR3α,‬ ‭CDR1β,‬‭CDR2β‬‭and‬
‭CDR3β‬ ‭sequences‬ ‭were‬ ‭embedded‬ ‭by‬ ‭a‬ ‭BLOSUM50‬ ‭encoding‬ ‭scheme‬ ‭(Henikoff‬ ‭and‬
‭Henikoff‬ ‭1992)‬‭,‬ ‭mapping‬ ‭each‬ ‭unique‬ ‭residue‬ ‭into‬ ‭a‬ ‭vector‬ ‭of‬ ‭20‬ ‭entries.‬ ‭That‬ ‭is,‬ ‭the‬
‭embeddings‬‭are‬‭matrices‬‭with‬‭an‬‭embedding‬‭dimension‬‭of‬‭size‬‭20‬‭and‬‭a‬‭residue‬‭dimension‬
‭of‬‭size‬‭equal‬‭to‬‭length‬‭of‬‭the‬‭sequence‬‭getting‬‭mapped.‬‭In‬‭alternative‬‭approaches,‬‭the‬‭CDRs‬
‭were‬ ‭embedded‬ ‭by‬ ‭various‬ ‭pretrained‬ ‭transformers,‬ ‭namely‬ ‭ESM-1b‬ ‭(Rives‬ ‭et‬ ‭al.‬‭2021)‬‭,‬
‭ProtT5-XL-U50‬‭(Elnaggar‬‭et‬‭al.‬‭2022)‬‭and‬‭the‬‭variant‬‭of‬‭ESM-2‬‭with‬‭650‬‭million‬‭parameters‬
‭(Z.‬‭Lin‬‭et‬‭al.‬‭2023)‬‭.‬‭These‬‭transformer‬‭embeddings‬‭were‬‭made‬‭by‬‭the‬‭following‬‭procedure:‬
‭First,‬‭embeddings‬‭of‬‭the‬‭α-‬‭and‬‭β-‬‭chain‬‭of‬‭TCR‬‭were‬‭made‬‭by‬‭inputting‬‭them‬‭one‬‭by‬‭one‬
‭into‬‭the‬‭transformer‬‭and‬‭extracting‬‭the‬‭output‬‭from‬‭the‬‭last‬‭layer‬‭of‬‭the‬‭transformer.‬‭Then,‬
‭embeddings‬‭corresponding‬‭to‬‭the‬‭amino‬‭acids‬‭of‬‭each‬‭CDR‬‭were‬‭cut‬‭out‬‭of‬‭the‬‭embeddings‬
‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭two‬ ‭chains.‬ ‭Just‬ ‭like‬ ‭the‬ ‭BLOSUM50‬ ‭embeddings,‬ ‭the‬ ‭resulting‬‭embeddings‬‭were‬
‭matrices‬‭with‬‭vector‬‭embeddings‬‭per‬‭residue.‬‭The‬‭embedding‬‭dimension‬‭size‬‭was‬‭1280‬‭for‬
‭ESM-1b‬ ‭and‬ ‭ESM-2‬ ‭and‬ ‭1024‬ ‭for‬ ‭ProtT5-XL-U50.‬ ‭Peptides‬ ‭were‬ ‭not‬ ‭embedded‬ ‭by‬
‭transformers,‬‭the‬‭reason‬‭being‬‭that‬‭all‬‭the‬‭tested‬‭transformers‬‭are‬‭intended‬‭to‬‭be‬‭used‬‭on‬‭full‬
‭protein sequences.‬
‭In‬‭order‬‭for‬‭the‬‭BLOSUM50‬‭and‬‭transformer‬‭embeddings‬‭to‬‭be‬‭compatible‬‭as‬‭input‬‭to‬‭neural‬
‭networks,‬ ‭all‬ ‭embeddings‬ ‭of‬ ‭a‬ ‭given‬ ‭feature‬ ‭(peptide‬ ‭and‬ ‭the‬ ‭different‬ ‭CDRs)‬ ‭were‬
‭right-padded‬‭to‬‭the‬‭maximum‬‭sequence‬‭length‬‭observed‬‭for‬‭that‬‭given‬‭feature‬‭in‬‭the‬‭dataset.‬
‭That‬ ‭is,‬ ‭vectors‬ ‭were‬ ‭added‬‭to‬‭the‬‭right‬‭of‬‭an‬‭embedding‬‭until‬‭its‬‭residue‬‭dimension‬‭size‬
‭became‬‭equal‬‭to‬‭the‬‭maximum‬‭residue‬‭dimension‬‭size‬‭observed‬‭for‬‭the‬‭given‬‭feature.‬‭For‬
‭the‬‭BLOSUM50‬‭embeddings,‬‭the‬‭entries‬‭of‬‭the‬‭padding‬‭vectors‬‭were‬‭all‬‭set‬‭to‬‭a‬‭value‬‭of‬‭-5,‬
‭while‬ ‭for‬ ‭the‬ ‭transformer‬ ‭embeddings‬ ‭a‬ ‭value‬ ‭of‬ ‭0‬ ‭was‬ ‭used‬ ‭instead.‬ ‭After‬ ‭padding,‬‭the‬
‭residue‬‭dimension‬‭size‬‭of‬‭the‬‭embeddings‬‭was‬‭12‬‭for‬‭peptides,‬‭7‬‭for‬‭CDR1α,‬‭8‬‭for‬‭CDR2α,‬
‭22 for CDR3α, 6 for CDR1β, 7 for CDR2β, and 23 for CDR3β.‬
‭The‬ ‭different‬ ‭embeddings‬ ‭were‬ ‭rescaled‬ ‭by‬ ‭matrix‬ ‭scalar‬ ‭multiplication.‬ ‭We‬ ‭will‬ ‭call‬ ‭the‬
‭scalar‬ ‭for‬ ‭a‬ ‭scaling‬‭factor.‬‭The‬‭scaling‬‭factor‬‭was‬‭1/5‬‭for‬‭all‬‭peptide‬‭embeddings.‬‭For‬‭the‬
‭CDR‬‭embeddings‬‭various‬‭scaling‬‭factors‬‭were‬‭tested,‬‭with‬‭the‬‭scaling‬‭factor‬‭being‬‭the‬‭same‬
‭across‬ ‭the‬ ‭different‬ ‭CDRs‬ ‭for‬ ‭a‬ ‭given‬ ‭model‬ ‭(table‬ ‭3.1).‬ ‭Due‬ ‭to‬ ‭rescaling,‬ ‭all‬ ‭peptide‬
‭embeddings‬ ‭would‬ ‭end‬ ‭up‬ ‭with‬ ‭a‬ ‭padding‬ ‭value‬ ‭of‬ ‭-1‬ ‭while‬ ‭the‬‭transformer‬‭embeddings‬
‭would‬ ‭still‬ ‭have‬ ‭a‬ ‭padding‬ ‭value‬ ‭of‬ ‭0.‬ ‭The‬ ‭final‬ ‭padding‬ ‭value‬ ‭of‬ ‭BLOSUM50‬ ‭CDR‬
‭embeddings depended on the used scaling factor.‬
‭27‬
==PAGE:32==
‭3.2.2 FNN Embeddings‬
‭Embeddings‬ ‭for‬ ‭the‬ ‭FNN‬‭architecture‬‭were‬‭derived‬‭from‬‭the‬‭CNN‬‭embeddings.‬‭While‬‭the‬
‭CDR‬‭embeddings‬‭for‬‭the‬‭CNN‬‭were‬‭matrices‬‭with‬‭vector‬‭embeddings‬‭per‬‭residue,‬‭the‬‭CDR‬
‭embeddings‬‭produced‬‭for‬‭the‬‭FNN‬‭were‬‭vector‬‭representations‬‭of‬‭the‬‭sequences.‬‭Each‬‭FNN‬
‭CDR‬‭embedding‬‭was‬‭created‬‭by‬‭taking‬‭the‬‭average‬‭over‬‭the‬‭embedding‬‭dimension‬‭of‬‭the‬
‭CNN‬‭embedding.‬‭This‬‭was‬‭only‬‭done‬‭for‬‭the‬‭ESM-1b‬‭embeddings‬‭with‬‭a‬‭scaling‬‭factor‬‭of‬
‭1/20,‬‭since‬‭they‬‭were‬‭the‬‭only‬‭ones‬‭that‬‭were‬‭going‬‭to‬‭be‬‭tested.‬‭Peptide‬‭embeddings‬‭for‬‭the‬
‭FNN‬‭were‬‭made‬‭differently.‬‭To‬‭create‬‭these‬‭embeddings,‬‭the‬‭CNN‬‭peptide‬‭embeddings‬‭were‬
‭collapsed into one dimension.‬
‭3.3 Model Architectures‬
‭A‬‭CNN‬‭(fig.‬‭3.1a)‬‭and‬‭a‬‭FNN‬‭(fig‬‭3.1b)‬‭were‬‭constructed‬‭in‬‭Keras‬‭(Cholet‬‭2015)‬‭.‬‭Both‬‭neural‬
‭networks‬‭take‬‭CDR1α,‬‭CDR2α,‬‭CDR3α,‬‭CDR1β,‬‭CDR2β‬‭and‬‭CDR3β‬‭of‬‭an‬‭embedded‬‭TCR‬
‭amino‬‭acid‬‭sequence‬‭as‬‭input‬‭as‬‭well‬‭as‬‭the‬‭embedded‬‭amino‬‭acid‬‭sequence‬‭of‬‭the‬‭peptide.‬
‭The‬ ‭output‬ ‭of‬ ‭both‬ ‭neural‬ ‭networks‬‭was‬‭the‬‭predicted‬‭score‬‭for‬‭belonging‬‭to‬‭the‬‭class‬‭of‬
‭binding‬ ‭TCR-peptide‬ ‭pairs,‬ ‭referred‬‭to‬‭hereafter‬‭as‬‭the‬‭binding‬‭score.‬‭All‬‭weights‬‭in‬‭both‬
‭architectures‬‭were‬‭initialized‬‭by‬‭the‬‭Glorot‬‭uniform‬‭initializer‬‭(Glorot‬‭and‬‭Bengio‬‭2010)‬‭,‬‭while‬
‭the‬ ‭bias‬ ‭parameters‬ ‭of‬ ‭convolutional‬‭layers‬‭(only‬‭present‬‭in‬‭the‬‭CNN‬‭architecture)‬‭and‬‭of‬
‭dense layers (present in both architectures) were initialized to zero.‬
‭3.3.1 CNN Architecture‬
‭Two‬ ‭differently‬ ‭sized‬ ‭CNN‬ ‭architectures‬ ‭were‬‭built‬‭(fig.‬‭3.1a);‬‭a‬‭1X‬‭architecture‬‭and‬‭a‬‭2X‬
‭architecture‬ ‭with‬ ‭twice‬ ‭the‬ ‭amount‬ ‭of‬ ‭convolutional‬ ‭filters‬ ‭and‬ ‭hidden‬ ‭neurons.‬ ‭For‬ ‭both‬
‭architectures‬ ‭sizes,‬ ‭the‬ ‭embedded‬ ‭input‬ ‭sequences‬ ‭are‬ ‭processed‬ ‭separately‬‭by‬‭each‬‭of‬
‭their‬ ‭own‬ ‭one-dimensional‬ ‭convolutional‬ ‭layers,‬‭followed‬‭by‬‭each‬‭of‬‭their‬‭own‬‭global‬‭max‬
‭pooling‬‭layers.‬‭The‬‭kernel‬‭sizes‬‭and‬‭number‬‭of‬‭filters‬‭for‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭layer‬‭are‬‭identical‬
‭for‬ ‭all‬ ‭input‬ ‭features.‬ ‭Slices‬ ‭over‬ ‭the‬ ‭embedding‬ ‭dimension‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭sequences‬‭are‬‭fed‬‭as‬
‭different‬‭input‬‭channels‬‭to‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭layer‬‭where‬‭convolutional‬‭filters‬‭with‬‭kernel‬‭sizes‬
‭of‬‭1,‬‭3,‬‭5,‬‭7‬‭and‬‭9‬‭are‬‭convolved‬‭with‬‭the‬‭input‬‭over‬‭the‬‭amino‬‭acid‬‭dimension.‬‭For‬‭each‬‭of‬
‭the‬‭different‬‭kernel‬‭sizes‬‭we‬‭have‬‭16‬‭(1X)‬‭or‬‭32‬‭(2X)‬‭filters‬‭of‬‭the‬‭same‬‭size‬‭(see‬‭table‬‭3.1)‬
‭giving‬‭a‬‭total‬‭of‬‭80‬‭or‬‭160‬‭filters‬‭per‬‭sequence.‬‭The‬‭convolutional‬‭layer‬‭uses‬‭a‬‭stride‬‭length‬‭of‬
‭1‬‭and‬‭zero‬‭padding‬‭applied‬‭evenly‬‭to‬‭both‬‭sides‬‭of‬‭the‬‭input‬‭sequence,‬‭such‬‭that‬‭the‬‭output‬
‭gets‬‭the‬‭same‬‭spatial‬‭dimension‬‭(the‬‭amino‬‭acid‬‭dimension)‬‭as‬‭the‬‭input.‬‭The‬‭outputs‬‭from‬
‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭layer‬‭of‬‭the‬‭peptide‬‭are‬‭activated‬‭by‬‭a‬‭ReLU‬‭activation‬‭function‬‭while‬‭the‬
‭outputs‬‭from‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭layers‬‭of‬‭the‬‭CDRs‬‭are‬‭activated‬‭by‬‭either‬‭ReLU‬‭or‬‭sigmoid‬
‭depending‬‭on‬‭the‬‭model‬‭(table‬‭3.1).‬‭In‬‭the‬‭global‬‭max‬‭pooling‬‭layer,‬‭the‬‭activated‬‭outputs‬
‭from‬ ‭the‬ ‭convolution‬ ‭layers‬ ‭are‬ ‭reduced‬ ‭to‬ ‭a‬ ‭single‬ ‭value‬ ‭for‬‭each‬‭filter,‬‭giving‬‭a‬‭feature‬
‭vector.‬‭Feature‬‭vectors‬‭resulting‬‭from‬‭the‬‭different‬‭input‬‭sequences‬‭are‬‭then‬‭concatenated‬
‭into‬ ‭a‬ ‭single‬ ‭vector‬ ‭of‬ ‭560‬ ‭(1X)‬ ‭or‬ ‭1120‬ ‭(2X)‬ ‭entries.‬ ‭Dropout‬ ‭is‬ ‭then‬ ‭applied‬ ‭to‬ ‭the‬
‭concatenated‬ ‭vector‬ ‭for‬ ‭some‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭models‬ ‭(table‬ ‭3.1)‬ ‭with‬ ‭a‬ ‭dropout‬ ‭rate‬ ‭of‬ ‭0.6.‬ ‭The‬
‭concatenated‬ ‭vector‬ ‭is‬ ‭then‬ ‭fed‬ ‭into‬ ‭a‬ ‭dense‬ ‭layer‬ ‭of‬ ‭either‬ ‭64‬ ‭(1X)‬ ‭or‬ ‭128‬‭(2X)‬‭hidden‬
‭neurons‬ ‭(table‬ ‭3.1)‬ ‭and‬ ‭finally‬ ‭into‬ ‭a‬ ‭dense‬ ‭layer‬‭of‬‭a‬‭single‬‭output‬‭neuron.‬‭The‬‭sigmoid‬
‭activation function is applied to both dense layers.‬
‭28‬
==PAGE:33==
‭3.3.2 FNN Architecture‬
‭The‬ ‭FNN‬ ‭architecture‬ ‭(fig.‬ ‭3.1b)‬ ‭is‬ ‭similar‬ ‭to‬ ‭the‬ ‭CNN,‬ ‭but‬ ‭without‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭and‬
‭global‬ ‭max‬ ‭pooling‬ ‭layer.‬ ‭The‬ ‭embedded‬‭input‬‭sequences‬‭are‬‭concatenated‬‭into‬‭a‬‭single‬
‭vector.‬‭Depending‬‭on‬‭the‬‭model‬‭(table‬‭3.1)‬‭dropout‬‭is‬‭then‬‭applied‬‭with‬‭a‬‭dropout‬‭rate‬‭of‬‭0.6.‬
‭The‬‭concatenated‬‭vector‬‭is‬‭then‬‭fed‬‭into‬‭a‬‭dense‬‭layer‬‭of‬‭64‬‭hidden‬‭neurons‬‭followed‬‭by‬‭a‬
‭dense‬‭layer‬‭of‬‭a‬‭single‬‭output‬‭neuron.‬‭Like‬‭for‬‭the‬‭CNN,‬‭the‬‭sigmoid‬‭activation‬‭function‬‭is‬
‭applied to both dense layers.‬
‭Fig.‬‭3.1.‬‭Model‬‭architectures.‬‭(a)‬‭CNN‬‭architecture.‬‭Embedded‬‭input‬‭sequences‬‭(CDRs‬‭and‬‭the‬‭peptide)‬‭are‬
‭processed‬ ‭separately‬ ‭by‬‭each‬‭their‬‭own‬‭one-dimensional‬‭convolutional‬‭layer,‬‭followed‬‭by‬‭each‬‭their‬‭own‬‭global‬
‭max‬‭pooling‬‭layer.‬‭Output‬‭from‬‭max‬‭pooling‬‭is‬‭concatenated‬‭into‬‭a‬‭single‬‭vector‬‭and‬‭dropout‬‭is‬‭applied‬‭depending‬
‭on‬‭the‬‭model‬‭(table‬‭3.1).‬‭The‬‭values‬‭of‬‭the‬‭vector‬‭are‬‭then‬‭passed‬‭through‬‭a‬‭dense‬‭layer‬‭of‬‭either‬‭64‬‭(1X)‬‭or‬‭128‬
‭(2X)‬‭hidden‬‭neurons‬‭and‬‭finally‬‭through‬‭a‬‭dense‬‭layer‬‭of‬‭a‬‭single‬‭output‬‭neuron‬‭giving‬‭the‬‭binding‬‭score.‬‭(b)‬‭FNN‬
‭architecture.‬ ‭Embedded‬ ‭input‬ ‭sequences‬ ‭(CDRs‬ ‭and‬ ‭the‬ ‭peptide)‬ ‭are‬ ‭concatenated‬ ‭into‬ ‭a‬ ‭single‬ ‭vector‬ ‭and‬
‭dropout‬‭is‬‭applied‬‭depending‬‭on‬‭the‬‭model‬‭(table‬‭3.1).‬‭The‬‭values‬‭of‬‭the‬‭vector‬‭are‬‭then‬‭passed‬‭through‬‭a‬‭dense‬
‭layer of 64 hidden neurons and finally through a dense layer of a single output neuron giving the binding score.‬
‭29‬
==PAGE:34==
‭3.4 Model Development‬
‭All‬‭models‬‭were‬‭developed‬‭using‬‭nested‬‭cross-validation‬‭with‬‭five‬‭outer‬‭folds‬‭and‬‭four‬‭inner‬
‭folds,‬ ‭giving‬ ‭20‬ ‭cross-validation‬ ‭models‬ ‭per‬ ‭model.‬ ‭Three‬ ‭of‬‭the‬‭five‬‭data‬‭partitions‬‭were‬
‭used for training and the remaining two were used for validation and testing, respectively.‬
‭Binary‬‭cross-entropy‬‭was‬‭used‬‭as‬‭a‬‭loss‬‭function‬‭and‬‭in‬‭most‬‭models,‬‭loss‬‭weighting‬‭was‬
‭applied‬ ‭(table‬‭3.1)‬‭such‬‭that‬‭the‬‭loss‬‭contribution‬‭from‬‭observations‬‭belonging‬‭to‬‭peptides‬
‭with‬ ‭fewer‬ ‭observations‬ ‭was‬ ‭weighted‬ ‭higher‬ ‭in‬ ‭the‬ ‭loss‬ ‭function.‬ ‭The‬ ‭weight‬
‭of‬ ‭an‬
‭𝑊‬‭𝑖‬‭ ‬
‭observation belonging to peptide‬ ‭is given by the‬‭formula:‬
‭𝑖‬
‭(3.1)‬
‭𝑊‬‭𝑖‬= ‭𝑙𝑜‬‭𝑔‬‭2‬
‭𝑇‬
‭𝑃‬‭𝑖‬
( ) · ‭𝑐‬
‭Where‬
‭is‬‭the‬‭total‬‭number‬‭of‬‭observations‬‭in‬‭the‬‭dataset,‬
‭is‬‭the‬‭number‬‭of‬‭observations‬
‭𝑇‬
‭𝑃‬‭𝑖‬
‭belonging‬‭to‬‭peptide‬ ‭and‬
‭is‬‭a‬‭constant‬‭that‬‭normalizes‬‭the‬‭weights,‬‭such‬‭that‬‭the‬‭overall‬
‭𝑖‬
‭𝑐‬
‭loss‬‭becomes‬‭close‬‭to‬‭that‬‭of‬‭the‬‭unweighted‬‭approach.‬‭From‬‭equation‬‭3.1‬‭it‬‭is‬‭observed‬‭that‬
‭will‬‭be‬‭large‬‭when‬
‭is‬‭small‬‭and‬‭vice‬‭versa.‬‭The‬‭formula‬‭for‬‭the‬‭normalizing‬‭constant‬
‭𝑊‬‭𝑖‬
‭𝑃‬‭𝑖‬
‭𝑐‬
‭is:‬
‭(3.2)‬
‭𝑐‬ =
‭𝑇‬
‭𝑗‬=‭1‬
‭𝑈‬
∑‭𝑙𝑜‬‭𝑔‬‭2‬
‭𝑇‬
‭𝑃‬‭𝑗‬
( )·‭𝑃‬‭𝑗‬
‭Where‬
‭is‬‭the‬‭number‬‭of‬‭observations‬‭belonging‬‭to‬‭peptide‬ ‭and‬
‭is‬‭the‬‭number‬‭of‬‭unique‬
‭𝑃‬‭𝑗‬
‭𝑗‬
‭𝑈‬
‭peptides. A derivation for equation 3.2 can be found in appendix A.1.‬
‭The‬‭value‬‭of‬‭the‬‭constant‬ ‭was‬‭determined‬‭to‬‭~0.26‬‭for‬‭our‬‭dataset.‬‭For‬‭models‬‭without‬‭loss‬
‭𝑐‬
‭weighting all weights were simply set to 1.‬
‭Neural‬‭network‬‭weights‬‭were‬‭updated‬‭during‬‭training‬‭with‬‭an‬‭Adam‬‭optimizer‬‭and‬‭a‬‭learning‬
‭rate‬‭of‬‭either‬‭0.001‬‭or‬‭0.0005‬‭(see‬‭table‬‭3.1).‬‭All‬‭models‬‭used‬‭a‬‭patience‬‭of‬‭100‬‭epochs‬‭and‬
‭validation‬ ‭AUC‬ ‭0.1‬ ‭as‬ ‭stopping‬ ‭criterion‬ ‭for‬ ‭early‬ ‭stopping.‬ ‭This‬ ‭means‬‭that‬‭training‬‭was‬
‭stopped‬‭after‬‭100‬‭epochs‬‭of‬‭no‬‭improvement‬‭in‬‭validation‬‭AUC‬‭0.1,‬‭saving‬‭the‬‭model‬‭state‬
‭with‬ ‭highest‬ ‭validation‬ ‭AUC‬ ‭0.1.‬ ‭The‬‭maximum‬‭number‬‭of‬‭epochs‬‭each‬‭model‬‭should‬‭be‬
‭trained‬‭for‬‭and‬‭the‬‭warm-up‬‭period‬‭were‬‭found‬‭by‬‭trial‬‭and‬‭error‬‭(table‬‭3.1).‬‭The‬‭warm-up‬
‭period‬ ‭is‬ ‭the‬ ‭number‬ ‭of‬ ‭epochs‬ ‭to‬ ‭wait‬ ‭before‬ ‭starting‬ ‭to‬ ‭monitor‬ ‭improvement‬ ‭for‬ ‭early‬
‭stopping.‬‭Thus‬‭training‬‭will‬‭not‬‭be‬‭stopped‬‭during‬‭the‬‭warm-up‬‭period.‬‭It‬‭was‬‭added‬‭to‬‭some‬
‭of‬‭the‬‭models‬‭due‬‭to‬‭the‬‭fact‬‭that‬‭they‬‭were‬‭slow‬‭at‬‭starting‬‭to‬‭learn.‬‭The‬‭maximum‬‭number‬
‭of‬ ‭epochs‬ ‭was‬ ‭generally‬ ‭set‬ ‭high‬ ‭so‬ ‭that‬ ‭training‬ ‭would‬ ‭be‬ ‭stopped‬ ‭by‬ ‭early‬ ‭stopping,‬
‭meaning‬‭that‬‭training‬‭would‬‭generally‬‭be‬‭stopped‬‭when‬‭no‬‭improvement‬‭was‬‭seen.‬ ‭Finally,‬
‭the‬ ‭training‬ ‭data‬ ‭was‬ ‭shuffled‬ ‭and‬ ‭divided‬ ‭into‬ ‭batches‬ ‭of‬ ‭64‬ ‭observations‬ ‭before‬ ‭each‬
‭epoch.‬
‭30‬
==PAGE:35==
‭Table‬ ‭3.1.‬ ‭Overview‬ ‭over‬‭models.‬‭The‬‭columns‬‭are;‬‭the‬‭model‬‭index,‬‭the‬‭model‬‭architecture‬‭(CNN/FNN),‬‭the‬
‭used‬‭CDR‬‭embedding,‬‭whether‬‭the‬‭model‬‭uses‬‭loss‬‭weighting‬‭or‬‭not,‬‭whether‬‭the‬‭model‬‭uses‬‭dropout‬‭or‬‭not,‬‭the‬
‭architecture‬ ‭size‬ ‭(based‬ ‭on‬ ‭the‬‭number‬‭of‬‭convolution‬‭filters‬‭and‬‭hidden‬‭units),‬‭the‬‭learning‬‭rate,‬‭the‬‭activation‬
‭function‬‭of‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭layer‬‭for‬‭CDRs,‬‭the‬‭scaling‬‭factor‬‭used‬‭to‬‭scale‬‭the‬‭CDR‬‭embedding,‬‭the‬‭warm-up‬
‭period and the maximum allowed number of epochs to train a model for.‬

‭3.5 Performance Evaluation‬
‭In‬ ‭nested‬ ‭cross-validation,‬ ‭four‬ ‭models‬ ‭were‬ ‭generated‬ ‭per‬ ‭test‬ ‭set‬ ‭due‬ ‭to‬ ‭four‬ ‭different‬
‭combinations‬‭of‬‭training‬‭sets‬‭for‬‭each‬‭selected‬‭test‬‭set.‬‭The‬‭test‬‭set‬‭predictions‬‭of‬‭binding‬
‭probability‬ ‭between‬ ‭TCR‬ ‭and‬ ‭peptide‬ ‭were‬ ‭determined‬ ‭for‬‭each‬‭entry‬‭in‬‭a‬‭test‬‭set‬‭as‬‭an‬
‭average‬‭over‬‭the‬‭predictions‬‭resulting‬‭from‬‭the‬‭four‬‭different‬‭models.‬‭That‬‭is‬‭the‬‭four‬‭models‬
‭were‬‭used‬‭in‬‭an‬‭ensemble‬‭to‬‭get‬‭the‬‭test‬‭set‬‭predictions.‬‭Test‬‭performance‬‭was‬‭evaluated‬‭on‬
‭the‬‭five‬‭concatenated‬‭test‬‭sets‬‭in‬‭terms‬‭of‬‭three‬‭metrics‬‭AUC,‬‭AUC‬‭0.1‬‭and‬‭PPV‬‭in‬‭a‬‭per‬
‭peptide‬ ‭manner.‬ ‭A‬ ‭weighted‬ ‭and‬ ‭unweighted‬ ‭average‬ ‭over‬ ‭the‬‭per‬‭peptide‬‭performances‬
‭were computed as well:‬
‭(3.3)‬
‭𝑀‬‭𝑢‬=

‭is‬ ‭the‬
‭𝑀‬‭𝑢‬
‭𝑀‬‭𝑤‬
‭𝑀‬‭𝑖‬
‭performance‬‭of‬‭peptide‬ ‭,‬
‭is‬‭the‬‭number‬‭of‬‭unique‬‭peptides,‬
‭is‬‭the‬‭number‬‭of‬‭positive‬

‭𝑄‬‭𝑖‬

‭peptides.‬
‭The‬‭validation‬‭set‬‭predictions‬‭were‬‭not‬‭produced‬‭from‬‭an‬‭ensemble‬‭of‬‭models.‬‭Instead‬‭four‬
‭different‬ ‭predictions‬ ‭were‬ ‭made‬ ‭for‬ ‭each‬ ‭entry‬ ‭in‬ ‭a‬ ‭validation‬ ‭set‬ ‭resulting‬ ‭from‬ ‭the‬ ‭four‬
‭different‬ ‭cross-validation‬ ‭models‬ ‭per‬ ‭validation‬ ‭set.‬ ‭Validation‬ ‭performance‬ ‭was‬ ‭then‬
‭evaluated‬‭on‬‭each‬‭of‬‭the‬‭four‬‭sets‬‭of‬‭predictions‬‭for‬‭each‬‭validation‬‭set‬‭again‬‭in‬‭terms‬‭of‬‭the‬
‭three‬‭metrics‬‭AUC,‬‭AUC‬‭0.1‬‭and‬‭PPV.‬‭The‬‭performance‬‭was‬‭determined‬‭as‬‭the‬‭unweighted‬
‭average‬‭over‬‭the‬‭per‬‭peptide‬‭performances‬‭(equation‬‭3.3).‬‭For‬‭each‬‭metric‬‭20‬‭unweighted‬
‭average performances were calculated - one for each of the 20 cross-validation models.‬
‭3.6 Performance Comparison‬
‭To‬‭compare‬‭the‬‭test‬‭performance‬‭between‬‭two‬‭models‬‭an‬‭exact‬‭binomial‬‭test‬‭was‬‭carried‬‭out‬
‭at‬ ‭0.05‬ ‭level‬ ‭of‬ ‭significance.‬ ‭Here,‬ ‭the‬ ‭number‬ ‭of‬ ‭successes‬ ‭were‬ ‭considered‬ ‭to‬ ‭be‬ ‭the‬
‭number‬ ‭of‬ ‭peptides‬ ‭for‬ ‭which‬ ‭the‬ ‭first‬ ‭model‬ ‭had‬ ‭a‬ ‭strictly‬ ‭greater‬ ‭per‬ ‭peptide‬ ‭test‬
‭performance‬‭in‬‭terms‬‭of‬‭a‬‭specific‬‭metric‬‭than‬‭the‬‭second‬‭model.‬‭The‬‭null‬‭hypothesis‬‭was‬
‭that‬‭the‬‭probability‬‭of‬‭success‬‭was‬‭0.5‬‭or‬‭less,‬‭corresponding‬‭to‬‭that‬‭the‬‭first‬‭model‬‭had‬‭a‬
‭better‬ ‭performance‬ ‭than‬ ‭the‬ ‭second‬ ‭model‬ ‭for‬ ‭half‬ ‭or‬‭less‬‭than‬‭half‬‭of‬‭the‬‭peptides.‬‭The‬
‭alternative‬ ‭hypothesis‬ ‭was‬ ‭that‬ ‭the‬ ‭probability‬ ‭of‬ ‭success‬ ‭was‬ ‭greater‬ ‭than‬ ‭0.5,‬
‭corresponding‬‭to‬‭that‬‭the‬‭first‬‭model‬‭had‬‭a‬‭better‬‭performance‬‭than‬‭the‬‭second‬‭model‬‭for‬
‭more than half of the peptides.‬
‭3.7 Code Availability‬
‭Scripts produced for this project are available at:‬‭https://github.com/9las/masters_thesis‬
‭33‬
==PAGE:38==
‭4 Results‬
‭I‬‭set‬‭out‬‭to‬‭investigate‬‭how‬‭well‬‭NetTCR‬‭models‬‭using‬‭learned‬‭CDR‬‭embeddings‬‭perform‬
‭compared‬ ‭to‬ ‭a‬ ‭baseline‬ ‭NetTCR‬ ‭model‬ ‭using‬ ‭BLOSUM50‬ ‭embeddings‬ ‭for‬ ‭the‬ ‭CDR‬
‭sequences.‬‭The‬‭models‬‭were‬‭benchmarked‬‭based‬‭on‬‭their‬‭ability‬‭to‬‭correctly‬‭predict‬‭binding‬
‭of TCR to peptide:MHC molecules.‬
‭First‬ ‭the‬ ‭baseline‬ ‭model‬ ‭was‬‭set‬‭up‬‭by‬‭reproducing‬‭results‬‭from‬‭a‬‭colleague‬‭in‬‭the‬‭same‬
‭research‬‭group.‬‭Then‬‭models‬‭with‬‭different‬‭CDR‬‭embeddings,‬‭namely‬‭ESM-1b,‬‭ESM-2‬‭and‬
‭ProtT5-XL-U50‬ ‭were‬ ‭compared‬ ‭to‬ ‭the‬ ‭baseline‬ ‭model.‬ ‭Parameters‬ ‭were‬ ‭at‬ ‭first‬ ‭kept‬ ‭the‬
‭same‬‭as‬‭for‬‭the‬‭baseline‬‭model,‬‭but‬‭then‬‭the‬‭impact‬‭of‬‭scaling‬‭the‬‭embeddings‬‭with‬‭different‬
‭scaling‬‭factors‬‭and‬‭applying‬‭a‬‭different‬‭activation‬‭function‬‭(sigmoid)‬‭to‬‭the‬‭convolutional‬‭layer‬
‭for CDRs were investigated.‬
‭Due‬‭to‬‭the‬‭new‬‭embeddings‬‭being‬‭larger‬‭than‬‭the‬‭BLOSUM50‬‭embedding,‬‭they‬‭were‬‭likely‬
‭more‬‭information‬‭rich.‬‭It‬‭was‬‭therefore‬‭speculated‬‭that‬‭the‬‭new‬‭models‬‭would‬‭gain‬‭from‬‭a‬
‭larger‬ ‭architecture‬ ‭size.‬ ‭Models‬ ‭with‬ ‭double‬ ‭the‬ ‭number‬ ‭of‬ ‭hidden‬ ‭units‬ ‭and‬ ‭convolution‬
‭filters were therefore tested.‬
‭Finally‬‭it‬‭was‬‭also‬‭tested‬‭if‬‭the‬‭CNN‬‭architecture‬‭of‬‭NetTCR‬‭could‬‭be‬‭reduced‬‭to‬‭a‬‭simpler‬
‭FNN architecture without having a negative impact on performance.‬
‭4.1 Reproduce Earlier Results‬
‭To‬ ‭get‬ ‭a‬ ‭baseline‬ ‭model,‬ ‭the‬ ‭first‬ ‭objective‬ ‭for‬ ‭the‬ ‭project‬ ‭was‬ ‭to‬ ‭reproduce‬ ‭a‬ ‭model‬
‭performance‬‭plot‬‭of‬‭a‬‭colleague‬‭in‬‭the‬‭same‬‭research‬‭group.‬‭For‬‭this,‬‭a‬‭NetTCR‬‭CNN‬‭model‬
‭using‬ ‭BLOSUM50‬ ‭embedded‬ ‭sequences‬ ‭together‬ ‭with‬ ‭a‬ ‭data‬ ‭set‬ ‭consisting‬ ‭of‬ ‭TCR‬
‭sequences,‬ ‭peptides‬ ‭and‬ ‭information‬ ‭of‬ ‭if‬ ‭the‬ ‭TCRs‬ ‭bind‬ ‭to‬ ‭a‬ ‭given‬ ‭peptide‬ ‭or‬ ‭not‬ ‭was‬
‭applied.‬‭The‬‭impact‬‭of‬‭using‬‭dropout,‬‭as‬‭well‬‭as‬‭weighting‬‭peptides‬‭with‬‭fewer‬‭observations‬
‭higher‬‭in‬‭the‬‭loss‬‭function‬‭was‬‭analyzed,‬‭resulting‬‭in‬‭four‬‭models;‬‭a‬‭model‬‭that‬‭uses‬‭neither‬
‭dropout‬‭nor‬‭weighting,‬‭a‬‭model‬‭that‬‭uses‬‭dropout‬‭only,‬‭a‬‭model‬‭that‬‭uses‬‭weighting‬‭only‬‭and‬
‭a‬ ‭model‬ ‭that‬ ‭uses‬ ‭both‬ ‭dropout‬ ‭and‬ ‭weighting.‬ ‭Their‬ ‭model‬ ‭IDs‬ ‭are‬ ‭4,‬ ‭3,‬ ‭2‬ ‭and‬ ‭1,‬
‭respectively‬ ‭(see‬ ‭table‬ ‭3.1).‬ ‭Since‬ ‭all‬‭models‬‭in‬‭the‬‭project‬‭were‬‭developed‬‭using‬‭nested‬
‭cross-validation‬ ‭with‬ ‭five‬‭outer‬‭folds‬‭and‬‭four‬‭inner‬‭folds,‬‭20‬‭cross-validation‬‭models‬‭were‬
‭obtained‬ ‭for‬ ‭each‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭four‬ ‭models.‬ ‭The‬ ‭performance‬ ‭of‬ ‭the‬ ‭models‬‭was‬‭measured‬‭by‬
‭AUC, AUC 0.1 and PPV.‬
‭Of‬ ‭these‬ ‭measures,‬ ‭AUC‬ ‭0.1‬ ‭was‬ ‭the‬ ‭primary‬ ‭metric‬ ‭of‬ ‭interest‬ ‭to‬ ‭optimize.‬ ‭This‬ ‭metric‬
‭corresponds‬‭to‬‭the‬‭area‬‭under‬‭the‬‭ROC‬‭curve‬‭up‬‭to‬‭a‬‭false‬‭positive‬‭rate‬‭(FPR)‬‭of‬‭0.1,‬‭and‬
‭focuses‬‭on‬‭the‬‭true‬‭positive‬‭rate‬‭(TPR)‬‭of‬‭the‬‭models‬‭at‬‭a‬‭low‬‭FPR.‬‭This‬‭constraint‬‭in‬‭FPR‬
‭means‬‭that‬‭when‬‭we‬‭optimize‬‭the‬‭AUC‬‭0.1‬‭we‬‭optimize‬‭the‬‭TPR‬‭while‬‭keeping‬‭the‬‭number‬‭of‬
‭false‬ ‭positives‬ ‭low,‬ ‭which‬ ‭is‬ ‭often‬ ‭of‬ ‭clinical‬ ‭interest.‬ ‭The‬ ‭secondary‬ ‭metric‬‭of‬‭interest‬‭to‬
‭optimize‬ ‭was‬ ‭PPV‬ ‭as‬ ‭it‬ ‭also‬ ‭measures‬ ‭the‬ ‭performance‬‭for‬‭low‬‭FPR.‬‭More‬‭specifically‬‭it‬
‭constraints‬‭FPR‬‭to‬‭be‬‭below‬‭0.2‬‭for‬‭our‬‭dataset‬‭where‬‭we‬‭have‬‭five‬‭times‬‭as‬‭many‬‭negative‬
‭observations‬‭as‬‭positive‬‭ones‬‭(see‬‭section‬‭2.3.3).‬‭The‬‭least‬‭important‬‭metric‬‭to‬‭optimize‬‭was‬
‭AUC‬‭since‬‭it‬‭has‬‭no‬‭constraint‬‭on‬‭the‬‭FPR.‬‭Since‬‭AUC‬‭0.1‬‭was‬‭the‬‭primary‬‭metric‬‭of‬‭interest,‬
‭validation‬‭AUC‬‭0.1‬‭was‬‭used‬‭as‬‭a‬‭stopping‬‭criterion‬‭for‬‭early‬‭stopping‬‭when‬‭training‬‭each‬‭of‬
‭the cross-validation models.‬
‭This‬‭is‬‭visualized‬‭in‬‭fig.‬‭4.1‬‭and‬‭4.2,‬‭which‬‭portray‬‭the‬‭learning‬‭curves‬‭for‬‭AUC‬‭0.1‬‭and‬‭loss,‬
‭respectively, in the model incorporating both dropout and loss weighting.‬

‭validation‬ ‭learning‬ ‭curves‬ ‭are‬ ‭shown‬ ‭for‬ ‭all‬ ‭20‬ ‭cross-validation‬ ‭models.‬ ‭For‬ ‭each‬ ‭cross-validation‬ ‭model‬ ‭the‬
‭vertical‬‭line‬‭marks‬‭the‬‭best‬‭found‬‭model‬‭state‬‭based‬‭on‬‭validation‬‭AUC‬‭0.1‬‭before‬‭training‬‭was‬‭halted‬‭due‬‭to‬‭early‬

‭learning‬ ‭curves‬ ‭are‬ ‭shown‬ ‭for‬ ‭all‬ ‭20‬ ‭cross-validation‬ ‭models.‬ ‭For‬ ‭each‬ ‭cross-validation‬‭model‬‭the‬‭vertical‬‭line‬
‭marks‬ ‭the‬ ‭best‬ ‭found‬ ‭model‬ ‭state‬‭based‬‭on‬‭validation‬‭AUC‬‭0.1‬‭(see‬‭fig.‬‭4.1)‬‭before‬‭training‬‭was‬‭halted‬‭due‬‭to‬
‭early stopping.‬

‭The‬‭test‬‭performance‬‭of‬‭the‬‭four‬‭trained‬‭models‬‭was‬‭compared‬‭by‬‭AUC‬‭0.1‬‭(fig.‬‭4.3)‬‭as‬‭well‬
‭as‬‭AUC‬‭(fig.‬‭B.1)‬‭and‬‭PPV‬‭(fig‬‭B.2).‬‭To‬‭better‬‭see‬‭the‬‭difference‬‭in‬‭performance‬‭between‬‭the‬
‭models the delta test performance was plotted as well (fig. 4.4, B.3-4).‬
‭Fig.‬ ‭4.3.‬ ‭Effect‬ ‭of‬ ‭dropout‬ ‭and‬ ‭loss‬ ‭weighting‬ ‭on‬ ‭model‬ ‭test‬ ‭performance‬ ‭measured‬ ‭by‬ ‭AUC‬ ‭0.1.‬
‭Combinations‬ ‭of‬‭including/excluding‬‭dropout‬‭and‬‭loss‬‭weighting‬‭results‬‭in‬‭four‬‭tested‬‭models,‬‭each‬‭shown‬‭by‬‭a‬
‭different‬ ‭color.‬ ‭The‬ ‭per‬ ‭peptide‬ ‭performance‬ ‭is‬ ‭shown‬ ‭to‬ ‭the‬ ‭right,‬ ‭where‬ ‭peptides‬ ‭are‬ ‭sorted‬ ‭from‬ ‭the‬ ‭most‬
‭abundant‬ ‭peptide‬ ‭to‬‭the‬‭least‬‭abundant‬‭peptide‬‭only‬‭counting‬‭positive‬‭peptides.‬‭The‬‭mean‬‭and‬‭weighted‬‭mean‬
‭performance‬‭taken‬‭over‬‭all‬‭peptides‬‭is‬‭shown‬‭to‬‭the‬‭left,‬‭where‬‭the‬‭weighted‬‭mean‬‭performance‬‭weights‬‭peptides‬
‭after‬ ‭their‬ ‭occurrence.‬ ‭Numbers‬ ‭in‬ ‭parentheses‬ ‭in‬ ‭labels‬ ‭along‬ ‭the‬ ‭first‬ ‭axis‬ ‭are‬ ‭the‬ ‭occurrence‬ ‭of‬ ‭positive‬
‭peptides in each group.‬

‭Fig.‬ ‭4.4.‬ ‭Effect‬ ‭of‬ ‭dropout‬ ‭and‬ ‭loss‬ ‭weighting‬ ‭on‬ ‭model‬ ‭test‬ ‭performance‬ ‭measured‬ ‭by‬ ‭ΔAUC‬ ‭0.1.‬
‭Combinations‬‭of‬‭including/excluding‬‭dropout‬‭and‬‭loss‬‭weighting‬‭results‬‭in‬‭four‬‭tested‬‭models,‬‭where‬‭three‬‭of‬‭them‬
‭are‬‭shown‬‭by‬‭a‬‭different‬‭color.‬‭The‬‭performance‬‭of‬‭the‬‭fourth‬‭model‬‭with‬‭no‬‭dropout‬‭and‬‭no‬‭loss‬‭weighting‬‭is‬‭used‬
‭as‬‭the‬‭origin‬‭and‬‭the‬‭performance‬‭of‬‭the‬‭other‬‭three‬‭models‬‭are‬‭given‬‭as‬‭ΔAUC‬‭0.1‬‭relative‬‭to‬‭this‬‭origin.‬‭The‬‭per‬

‭peptide‬‭to‬‭the‬‭least‬‭abundant‬‭peptide‬‭only‬‭counting‬‭positive‬‭peptides.‬‭The‬‭difference‬‭in‬‭mean‬‭and‬‭weighted‬‭mean‬
‭performance‬‭taken‬‭over‬‭all‬‭peptides‬‭is‬‭shown‬‭to‬‭the‬‭left,‬‭where‬‭the‬‭weighted‬‭mean‬‭performance‬‭weights‬‭peptides‬
‭after‬ ‭their‬ ‭occurrence.‬ ‭Numbers‬ ‭in‬ ‭parentheses‬ ‭in‬ ‭labels‬ ‭along‬ ‭the‬ ‭first‬ ‭axis‬ ‭are‬ ‭the‬ ‭occurrence‬ ‭of‬ ‭positive‬
‭peptides in each group.‬
‭From‬‭the‬‭plots,‬‭it‬‭is‬‭clear‬‭that‬‭the‬‭model‬‭with‬‭dropout‬‭and‬‭loss‬‭weighting‬‭performed‬‭the‬‭best.‬
‭It‬‭has‬‭a‬‭higher‬‭mean‬‭and‬‭weighted‬‭mean‬‭performance‬‭than‬‭the‬‭other‬‭three‬‭models‬‭across‬‭all‬
‭performance‬‭measures.‬‭The‬‭only‬‭exception‬‭is‬‭that‬‭the‬‭weighted‬‭mean‬‭AUC‬‭is‬‭a‬‭little‬‭lower‬
‭than‬‭for‬‭the‬‭model‬‭with‬‭dropout‬‭only.‬‭This‬‭aligns‬‭with‬‭the‬‭fact‬‭that‬‭the‬‭model‬‭with‬‭dropout‬‭and‬
‭loss‬‭weighting‬‭was‬‭found‬‭to‬‭perform‬‭significantly‬‭better‬‭than‬‭the‬‭model‬‭with‬‭only‬‭dropout‬‭in‬
‭terms‬‭of‬‭both‬‭AUC‬‭0.1‬‭(‬
‭)‬‭and‬‭PPV‬‭(‬
‭)‬‭but‬‭there‬‭was‬‭not‬‭found‬‭a‬‭significant‬

‭weighting‬ ‭was‬ ‭found‬ ‭to‬ ‭perform‬ ‭significantly‬ ‭better‬ ‭than‬ ‭the‬ ‭model‬‭with‬‭only‬‭weighting‬‭in‬

‭performance‬ ‭boost‬ ‭over‬ ‭the‬ ‭model‬ ‭without‬ ‭dropout‬ ‭and‬ ‭weighting.‬ ‭Specifically,‬ ‭both‬ ‭the‬
